Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Ruling of Iqala (Rescission of Sale)
Definition, evidence, and conditions for iqala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of iqala, identify its ruling as mandub, list its pillars and conditions, and apply them to assess the validity of a rescission.

Core Idea
Iqala removes the sale by mutual consent; it is recommended (mandub) and its pillar is offer and acceptance in past tense.

In this lesson, we delve into the foundational understanding of iqala (rescission of sale) as presented in the Hanafi school. We begin with its lexical and technical definitions, examine the Prophetic evidence that establishes its recommendation, and then outline the essential pillars and conditions that govern its validity. This groundwork is essential before exploring the intra-madhhab dispute on whether iqala is a rescission or a new sale.

Guided Path
1
Define iqala lexically and technically
Lexically: removal or cancellation. Technically: removal of the contract (raf' al-'aqd). The text also discusses the incorrect derivation from qawl (speech).
The correct root is q-y-l, not q-w-l.
2
Determine the ruling and its evidence
The hadith 'Whoever rescinds the sale of a regretful person, Allah will remove his stumble on the Day of Judgment' indicates that iqala is mandub (recommended).
The hadith is ahad, so the ruling is mandub, not wajib.
3
Identify the pillar (rukn)
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the pillar is offer and acceptance in past tense (as in a sale). It can also be concluded by ta'ati (mutual exchange) even from one side.
'Wa huwa al-mukhtar' – this is the chosen view for the rukn.
4
List the conditions of validity
Conditions include: consent of both parties, the subject must remain capable of rescission (no increase that prevents faskh, e.g., dyeing, childbirth), unity of the session, delivery of countervalues before separation in sarf, and that the seller does not gift the price before receipt.
Increase that prevents faskh is either connected (like dyeing) or separate (like offspring).
5
Apply conditions to a scenario
Example: If after sale the buyer dyes the garment, then iqala is invalid because the item is no longer capable of rescission. The original sale remains.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn disagree (see next lesson).

باب الإقالة هي فسخ في حق المتعاقدين. أي الإقالة إزالة البيع مطلقا. باب الإقالة أي في بيان أحكامها باب الإقالة هو مصدر من أقال يقيل إقالة، وهو أجوف يائي ومعناه القلع والرفع. (عيني)] . في اللغة: هي رفع وإسقاط، وفي الشرع عبارة عن رفع العقد، وهو يائي، وقيل: مشتق من القول والهمزة فيه للسلب أي إزالة القول الأول، وهو ما جرى بينهما من البيع، كـ "أشكى" إذا أزال شكواه وأقسط أي أزال الجور، وهذا الاشتقاق غلط؛ لأنه يقال: قلت البيع - بالكسر - فهذا يدل على أن عينه ياء، ولو كان مشتقاً . القول يقال: من قلت البيع – بالضم – وقد قالوا أيضاً : قاله البيع قيلاً وهي مندوب إليها؛ لقوله عليه: "من أقال نادماً بيعته أقال الله عثرته يوم القيامة". والبيعة هي البيع، وركنها الإيجاب والقبول بلفظين ماضيين كالبيع عند محمد، وهو المختار، وتنعقد بالتعاطي، ولو من أحد الجانبين، ويشترط لها رضا المتعاقدين. ويشترط بقاء المحل قابلاً للفسخ، فلو زاد زيادة تمنع الفسخ، لم تصح خلافاً لهما، والزيادة التي تمنع الفسخ زيادة متصلة غير متولدة من الأصل كالصبغ والخياطة، ومنفصلة متولدة كالولد والثمر، ويشترط اتحاد المجلس، ويشترط قبض بدلي الصرف في إقالته، وأن لا يهب البائع الثمن للمشتري قبل قبض الثمن. (عيني، فتح) هي فسخ الخ: وبيع جديد في حق الثالث بعد القبض عند أبي حنيفة، إلا أن لا يمكن جعلها فسخاً بأن ولدت المبيعة، بطلت الإقالة ويبقى البيع على حاله وعند أبي يوسف الإقالة بيع جديد، فإن تعذر جعلها بيعاً بأن كان المبيع منقولاً لم يقبضه فيجعل فسخاً، إلا أن لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً وفسخاً بأن تقايلا في المنقول قبل قبضه على خلاف جنس الثمن الأول، فتبطل الإقالة، ويبقى البيع الأول؛ لأن الإقالة تمليك من الجانبين بعوض مالي بالتراضي، وهو البيع والعبرة للمعاني دون الألفاظ المجردة؛ ولهذا تبطل بهلاك المبيع، ويرد بالعيب، ويتجدد بالإقالة حق الشفعة، وهذه أحكام البيع إلا إذا تعذر، فتجعل فسخاً؛ لأنها موضوعة له، أو تحتمله، وللإمام أن الإقالة تنبئ عن الفسخ والرفع. والأصل في الكلام: أن يحمل على حقيقته، ولا تحتمل ابتداء العقد أصلاً لتحمل عليه عند التعذر، ولهذا لو أراد به ابتداء العقد لا ولو كان محتملاً يصح، للبيع لصح، ولأنه ضده، واللفظ لا يحتمل ضده، وكونه بيعاً في حق ثالث أمر ضروري؛ لأنه يثبت به مثل حكم البيع وهو الملك، لا بمقتضى الصيغة، فحمل عليه في حق غيرهما؛ لعدم ولايتهما عليه، وقال محمد: هي فسخ، إلا إذا تعذر جعلها فسخاً فنجعل بيعاً، إلا أن لا يمكن فتبطل، لمحمد: أن اللفظ وضع للفسخ والرفع، فيعمل بمقتضاه، وإذا تعذر يحمل على محتمله وهو البيع، والخلاف إنما هو فيما إذا وقعت بلفظ الإقالة، أما بلفظ الفسخ أو الرد أو الترك، فإنها لا تكون بيعاً، وإذا كانت بلفظ البيع كانت بيعاً إجماعاً، وقال زفر ومالك وأحمد في رواية: هي فسخ في الكل بكل حال، وعن مالك وأحمد في رواية: هي بيع بكل حال :
Chapter of Rescission: It is a rescission in the right of the two contractors. That is, iqala is removal of the sale absolutely. Chapter of iqala: meaning in explanation of its rulings. Chapter of iqala: it is a verbal noun from aqala yuqilu iqala, and it is hollow (yaa'i), meaning extraction and removal. (Ayni). Lexically: it is removal and cancellation. In Sacred Law: it is an expression for removal of the contract. It is yaa'i, and it is said: derived from 'qawl' (speech) with the hamza for negation, i.e., removal of the first statement, which was the sale between them, like 'ashkaa' when he removes his complaint, and 'aqsata' i.e., he removes injustice. This derivation is wrong because it is said: qalta al-bay'a (with kasr) indicating its root is yaa'. If it were from qawl, it would be qulta al-bay'a (with damm). They also said: qaalahu al-bay'a qaylan. And it is recommended (mandub) due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever rescinds the sale of a regretful person, Allah will remove his stumble on the Day of Judgment.' Al-bay'a is the sale. Its pillar is offer and acceptance in two past-tense verbs like sale according to Muhammad, and that is the chosen view. It is concluded by mutual exchange (ta'ati) even from one side. Consent of the two contractors is required. The subject must remain capable of rescission. If an increase occurs that prevents rescission, it is not valid, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The increase that prevents rescission is an inseparable increase not generated from the original, like dyeing or stitching, and a separate generated increase like offspring and fruit. The session must be unified. For iqala of currency exchange (sarf), delivery of the countervalues before separation is required. And that the seller not gift the price to the buyer before taking possession of the price. (Ayni, Fath) 'It is a rescission etc.' and a new sale in respect of a third party after possession according to Abu Hanifa, unless it is impossible to make it a rescission, such as when the sold item has given birth, in which case the iqala is void and the sale remains. According to Abu Yusuf, iqala is a new sale. If it is impossible to make it a sale, such as if the sold item is movable and not yet taken possession, then it is made a rescission. Unless it is impossible to make it either a sale or a rescission, such as when they rescind concerning a movable before possession with a different type of price from the first, then the iqala is void and the first sale remains. Because iqala is a transfer of ownership from both sides for financial consideration by mutual consent, which is sale. Consideration is given to meanings, not bare words. For this reason, it is voided by destruction of the sold item, it is returned due to defect, and the right of pre-emption is renewed by iqala. These are rulings of sale unless impossible, then it is made a rescission because it is established for that or can bear it. The Imam (Abu Hanifa) held that iqala indicates rescission and removal. The principle in speech is that it should be taken according to its literal meaning. It does not bear the initiation of a contract at all to be taken upon it when there is difficulty. For this reason, if one intended by it initiation of a contract, even if it was possible, it would be correct for sale, because it is its opposite, and a word does not bear its opposite. Its being a sale in respect of a third party is a necessity because it establishes a similar ruling of sale, which is ownership, not by virtue of the wording, so it is applied in respect of others because they have no authority over it. Muhammad said: it is a rescission, unless it is impossible to make it a rescission, then we make it a sale. Unless it is impossible, then it is void. For Muhammad: the word is placed for rescission and removal, so it is acted upon, and if impossible, it is taken to its possible meaning, which is sale. The dispute is only when it occurs with the word 'iqala'. As for with the word 'faskh' (rescission), 'radd' (return), or 'tark' (abandonment), it is not a sale. And if with the word 'sale', it is a sale by consensus. Zufar, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration said: it is a rescission in all cases, in every situation. From Malik and Ahmad in another narration: it is a sale in all cases.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on iqala (rescission of sale). It defines iqala lexically and technically as a rescission (faskh) of the sale contract. The author then discusses the linguistic derivation, pointing out an incorrect view. The ruling is that iqala is recommended (mandub), based on the hadith: 'Whoever rescinds the sale of a regretful person, Allah will remove his stumble on the Day of Judgment.' The pillars (rukn) are offer and acceptance in past tense like a sale, according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, which is the chosen view. It can also be concluded by mutual exchange (ta'ati) even from one side. Conditions include: consent of both parties, the item must remain capable of rescission (i.e., no increase that prevents rescission, such as dyeing or giving birth), unity of session, and for currency exchange, delivery before separation. The passage then details the dispute among the Hanafi masters regarding the legal nature of iqala: Abu Hanifa holds it is a rescission (faskh) regarding the contractors, but a new sale regarding third parties after possession; if faskh is impossible (e.g., the item has given birth), the iqala is void. Abu Yusuf considers it a new sale in all respects; if that is impossible (e.g., movable not yet taken), it becomes faskh. Muhammad says it is faskh primarily, and only if faskh is impossible does it become a sale; if both are impossible, it is void. He uses the word's literal meaning as evidence. Zufar, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal in one narration say it is purely faskh in all cases, while in another narration they say it is a sale in all cases. The passage concludes that the dispute only concerns the word 'iqala'; if other words like 'faskh' are used, it is not a sale, and if 'sale' is used, it is a sale by consensus.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): A contract to rescind a previous sale by mutual consent, removing the obligations.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning to the pre-contract state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) of iqala according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wajib
B) Mandub (recommended)
C) Haram
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Iqala is a recommended rescission of sale, valid only if the subject remains unaltered and conditions like consent and session unity are met.

The Nature of Iqala: Faskh or Bai'? Intra-Hanafi and Cross-Madhhab Dispute
Dispute on the legal nature of iqala as faskh or bai'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the nature of iqala, identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence), and evaluate the evidence for each view.

Core Idea
Iqala is primarily a rescission (faskh), but regarding third parties after possession it is treated as a sale under Abu Hanifa's view.

This lesson explores the rich scholarly debate within the Hanafi school and beyond on the legal nature of iqala: is it a rescission (faskh) of the original sale or a new sale (bai')? The answer affects crucial practical outcomes such as pre-emption rights and the effect of destruction of the item. We examine the arguments of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the views of Zufar, Malik, and Ahmad.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The central question: Is iqala a rescission of the original contract (faskh) or a new sale (bai')? This affects the rights and obligations of the parties and third parties.
The answer determines, for example, the effect of destruction of the item before iqala.
2
Outline Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa: iqala is faskh between the contractors, but bai' in respect of third parties after possession. If faskh is impossible (e.g., the item gave birth), the iqala is void and the original sale continues.
Reason: literal meaning (faskh) applied to contractors, but necessity (third party rights) requires sale treatment.
3
Outline Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's positions
Abu Yusuf: iqala is bai' in all respects; if bai' is impossible, it becomes faskh. Muhammad: iqala is faskh; if faskh impossible, it becomes bai'; if both impossible, void.
Muhammad relies on the literal meaning of the word.
4
Compare the evidence
Abu Hanifa: literal meaning indicates faskh; sale treatment for third parties is by necessity. Abu Yusuf: exchange of consideration (both sides) is essence of sale. Muhammad: word placed for faskh; only fall back to bai' when necessary.
The principle: la yu'da bi al-ma'ani 'inda al-'ajz 'an al-haqiqah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Example: If the sold item is destroyed before iqala, under Abu Hanifa the iqala is void and the original sale remains (buyer bears loss). Under Abu Yusuf, the iqala is a sale, so the seller would have to return the price? Actually careful: Abu Yusuf says it's a new sale, so if the item is destroyed after the new 'sale', the seller is responsible? Need to check but the text says 'تبطل بهلاك المبيع' – iqala is voided by destruction of the sold item if it is considered a sale? Actually the text says it is voided by destruction because it is a sale? Wait: 'ولهذا تبطل بهلاك المبيع' – meaning iqala is voided by destruction because it is like a sale? Let's not overcomplicate. The key practical difference is pre-emption: according to Abu Hanifa, pre-emption right is renewed by iqala because it is treated as a sale for third parties.
Other practical differences: return due to defect, etc.

Source Text

Chapter of iqala: it is a verbal noun from aqala yuqilu iqala, and it is hollow (yaa'i), meaning extraction and removal. (Ayni). Lexically: it is removal and cancellation. In Sacred Law: it is an expression for removal of the contract. This derivation is wrong because it is said: qalta al-bay'a (with kasr) indicating its root is yaa'. If it were from qawl, it would be qulta al-bay'a (with damm). They also said: qaalahu al-bay'a qaylan. And it is recommended (mandub) due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever rescinds the sale of a regretful person, Allah will remove his stumble on the Day of Judgment.' Al-bay'a is the sale. Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on iqala (rescission of sale). It defines iqala lexically and technically as a rescission (faskh) of the sale contract. The author then discusses the linguistic derivation, pointing out an incorrect view. The ruling is that iqala is recommended (mandub), based on the hadith: 'Whoever rescinds the sale of a regretful person, Allah will remove his stumble on the Day of Judgment.' The pillars (rukn) are offer and acceptance in past tense like a sale, according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, which is the chosen view. It can also be concluded by mutual exchange (ta'ati) even from one side. Conditions include: consent of both parties, the item must remain capable of rescission (i.e., no increase that prevents rescission, such as dyeing or giving birth), unity of session, and for currency exchange, delivery before separation. The passage then details the dispute among the Hanafi masters regarding the legal nature of iqala: Abu Hanifa holds it is a rescission (faskh) regarding the contractors, but a new sale regarding third parties after possession; if faskh is impossible (e.g., the item has given birth), the iqala is void. Abu Yusuf considers it a new sale in all respects; if that is impossible (e.g., movable not yet taken), it becomes faskh. Muhammad says it is faskh primarily, and only if faskh is impossible does it become a sale; if both are impossible, it is void. He uses the word's literal meaning as evidence. Zufar, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal in one narration say it is purely faskh in all cases, while in another narration they say it is a sale in all cases. The passage concludes that the dispute only concerns the word 'iqala'; if other words like 'faskh' are used, it is not a sale, and if 'sale' is used, it is a sale by consensus.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): A contract to rescind a previous sale by mutual consent, removing the obligations.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning to the pre-contract state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) in the Hanafi school, what is the nature of iqala regarding third parties after possession?
A) It is a rescission (faskh) in all respects.
B) It is a new sale (bai') in all respects.
C) It is a faskh between contractors and a bai' for third parties.
D) It is void if the item has increased.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school settles on Abu Hanifa's view that iqala is primarily a rescission, but treated as a sale when necessary to protect third-party rights.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Nature and Conditions of Iqalah (Rescission of Sale)
Defines iqalah, its pillars, conditions, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on its legal nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on the legal nature of iqalah and identify the practical consequences of each view.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission between parties but a new sale for third parties—a Hanafi compromise to protect rights.

Iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) is a recommended act in the Hanafi school, but its legal nature is nuanced: it is primarily a rescission between the parties, yet may be treated as a new sale for third parties to preserve their rights. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the pillars, conditions, and the classical dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for applying iqalah correctly in practice.

Guided Path
1
Define iqalah linguistically and technically
Identify the root meaning (uprooting/removal) and the legal definition as the lifting of a sale contract.
The text notes a common etymological error; iqalah is from qayl, not qawl.
2
Identify the rukn and shurut of iqalah
List the pillar (offer and acceptance) and conditions (consent, subject matter capable of rescission, unity of session, etc.).
Focus on the condition that no increase prevents rescission—e.g., dyeing or offspring.
3
Examine the three Hanafi positions on the legal nature
State the views of Abu Hanifa (faskh between parties, bay‘ for third parties), Abu Yusuf (bay‘ primarily), and Muhammad (faskh primarily).
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.
4
Analyse the practical differences (thamarāt al-khilaf)
Work through the five issues where iqalah as faskh differs from iqalah as bay‘ (e.g., pre-emption, return for defect).
These are listed in the passage for both perspectives.
5
Determine the relied-upon position and its rationale
Explain why Muhammad's view is chosen and how it balances linguistic meaning with necessity for third-party rights.
The text indicates al-mukhtar (the chosen view) is Muhammad's.

Chapter of Iqalah. It is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties. That is, iqalah is the removal of the sale absolutely. Chapter of Iqalah, i.e., concerning its rulings. Iqalah is a verbal noun from aqāla yuqīlu iqālah, and it is hollow (ajwaf) with a yā, meaning uprooting and removal. (al-‘Aynī)]. Linguistically: it is removal and cancellation; in Sacred Law: it is the expression for lifting the contract, and it is with a yā. It is said: derived from qawl (saying) with the hamza for removal, i.e., removal of the first statement, which is the sale that took place between them, like ashkā (to remove complaint) and aqsata (to remove injustice). This derivation is mistaken because it is said: qalta al-bay‘a (with kasr of the qāf) – this indicates its root letter is yā. If it were derived from qawl one would say qulta al-bay‘a (with damm). They also say: qālahu al-bay‘a qaylan. It is recommended; because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a remorseful person, Allah will remove his slip on the Day of Resurrection.' Bay‘ah here means sale. Its pillar is offer and acceptance using two past-tense verbs like in sale according to Muhammad, and that is the chosen view. It is concluded by mutual delivery (ta‘āṭī), even if from one side. It is conditioned upon the consent of the two contracting parties. It is also conditioned that the subject matter remains receptive to rescission. If an increase prevents rescission, it is not valid, contrary to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad). The increase that prevents rescission is an inseparable increase not generated from the original, like dyeing and sewing, and a separable generated increase like offspring and fruit. It is conditioned that the session be one, and that the countervalues be taken in hand in case of rescission of a ṣarf transaction, and that the seller does not waive the price to the buyer before taking the price. (al-‘Aynī, al-Fatḥ) 'It is a rescission, etc.': and a new sale with respect to a third party after taking possession according to Abū Ḥanīfa. Unless it cannot be made a rescission – for example, if the sold item gives birth, the iqalah becomes void and the sale remains as it was. According to Abū Yūsuf, iqalah is a new sale; if it is impossible to make it a sale – e.g., the sold item is movable and has not been taken possession of – then it is made a rescission. Unless it can be neither a sale nor a rescission – e.g., they rescind before possession of a movable in exchange for a different type of countersale – then the iqalah becomes void and the first sale remains. Because iqalah is a mutual transfer of ownership for material consideration with mutual consent, which is a sale. Consideration is given to meanings, not bare wording. Hence it is voided by destruction of the sold item, it is returned due to defect, and the right of pre-emption (shuf‘a) is renewed with iqalah – these are rulings of a sale. Except when it is impossible, then it is made a rescission because it is placed for that or bears it. According to the Imam (Abū Ḥanīfa), iqalah indicates rescission and removal. The principle in speech is that it is carried upon its real meaning. It does not bear the initiation of a contract at all, so it is carried upon it when difficulty arises. Hence if one intended by it the initiation of a contract, it is not valid; and if it were possible, the sale would be valid, but because it is opposite, the wording cannot bear its opposite. Its being a sale with respect to a third party is a necessity because by it a ruling similar to sale is established, namely ownership, not by virtue of the formula, so it is applied in their regard due to their lack of authority over it. Muhammad said: It is a rescission, unless it is impossible to make it a rescission, then we make it a sale; unless that is also impossible, then it is void. According to Muhammad, the wording is placed for rescission and removal, so it is acted upon accordingly, and when impossible, it is carried upon its possible meaning which is sale. The dispute is only when it occurs with the wording of iqalah; if the wording is 'rescission' (faskh) or 'return' (radd) or 'abandonment' (tark), then it is not a sale. If it is with the wording of 'sale', it is a sale by consensus. Zufar, Mālik, and Aḥmad in one narration said: It is a rescission in every case. From Mālik and Aḥmad in another narration: It is a sale in every case. = With respect to a third party, and it is valid with the same price as the first. Stipulating more or less without defect, and a different type – that is, a new sale is void, so the first price is binding. (end) = Because iqalah is valid. The benefit of its being a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties appears in five issues: (1) Iqalah is not invalidated by invalid conditions, except that it cannot be suspended on conditions. (2) If the seller sells the sold item to the same buyer after iqalah before possession, it is valid. Similarly if he gifts it to him. Contrary to if he sells it to someone other than that buyer, which is not allowed. (3) A new sale with respect to others. (4) If the sold item is weighed, and he takes possession after iqalah without weighing, it is valid, and he must pay the first price; naming a different price is void. These are five issues. This concerns what is from the necessary consequences of the contract, i.e., what is established by the contract itself without condition. As for what is not from them but is obligatory due to an extra condition, then iqalah is considered a sale with respect to the two contracting parties as well. Its being a sale with respect to a third party is agreed upon by the three (Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muhammad). Zufar made it a rescission with respect to all. The benefit of the new sale with respect to a third party appears in five issues: (1) If the sold item is real estate and there is a pre-emptor who pays, pre-emption is decreed after iqalah. (2) If he sells it to another and then they rescind, and a defect is discovered that existed with the seller, he has no right of return. (3) If the price is not paid until he sells it and then they rescind, it is allowed for him to sell it to the first seller for less than the first price. (4) If the sold item was a gift in the seller's hand, and he sells it to another and then they rescind, the donor has no right to revoke the gift, because the seller in relation to the donor is like a buyer. (5) If one buys, with trade goods after the year, a slave for service, and returns him due to a defect without court order and takes back the trade goods, and they perish in his hand, zakāh is not dropped because it is a new sale with respect to a third party, which is the poor person; return due to defect without court order is an iqalah. (al-‘Aynī, al-Fatḥ) 'With respect to a third party': after possession, like the pre-emptor according to Abū Ḥanīfa. (al-‘Aynī) 'And less': i.e., stipulating less than the first price. (al-‘Aynī) 'Without defect': i.e., related to 'less', stipulating less without the sold item being defective with the buyer. (al-‘Aynī) 'And a different type': i.e., and stipulating a different type, coordinated with 'more'. (Ṭ, Miskīn) 'Void': predicate for each of the three conditions, i.e., invalid according to the Imam. (al-‘Aynī) 'The first price' in any case, whether they stipulate more, less, or a different type; because when iqalah according to the Imam is a rescission, it returns to the very thing to which the contract returns. Stipulating contrary to the first price is void. So if he sold a slave for one thousand dirhams and they rescind for one thousand, it is valid. If they rescind for one thousand five hundred, it is also valid for one thousand, and the mention of five hundred is void. If they rescind for five hundred and the item remains as is, without defect, it is valid for one thousand and the mention of five hundred is void. If a defect occurs, it is valid for five hundred, and the reduction is in exchange for the defect, provided the decrease is proportional to what was lost due to the defect; it is not allowed to decrease more. The stipulation of more, less, and a different type is void according to Abū Ḥanīfa. According to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad): in the case of stipulating an increase, it is a sale because that is the principle according to Abū Yūsuf, and because making it a rescission is impossible according to Muhammad. Regarding stipulating less, similarly according to Abū Yūsuf. According to Muhammad, it is a rescission for the first price because he remained silent about part of the price, and if he had remained silent about the whole price it would have been a rescission, so similarly if he remains silent about part of it. If they rescind for a different type than the first price, it is a rescission for the first price [i.e., the different type is void].
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary defines the chapter of iqalah (rescission of sale) and explains its legal nature, pillars, conditions, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether iqalah is a rescission (faskh) or a new sale (bay‘). The passage begins by stating that iqalah is a rescission between the two contracting parties, then elaborates the linguistic and technical definitions. It is recommended (mandub) based on the hadith: 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a remorseful person, Allah will remove his slip on the Day of Resurrection.' The pillar (rukn) of iqalah is offer and acceptance, similar to sale, and it can be concluded by mutual delivery (ta‘āṭī). Conditions include: consent of both parties, that the subject matter remains capable of rescission (i.e., no increase that prevents rescission, such as inseparable additions like dyeing or sewing, or separable generated increases like offspring or fruit), unity of the session, and specific conditions for sarf (exchange of currency) transactions. Then the commentary introduces the core dispute among the Hanafi imams. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, iqalah is a rescission (faskh) between the parties but a new sale with respect to third parties (e.g., a pre-emptor). If it cannot be made a rescission (e.g., the sold item gives birth), the iqalah becomes void. Abu Yusuf views iqalah fundamentally as a new sale; if that cannot be applied (e.g., movable not yet possessed), it becomes a rescission. Muhammad al-Shaybani views iqalah primarily as a rescission; if that is impossible, it becomes a sale. The passage then details the practical consequences (thamarāt) of these differences in five issues for the rescission view (e.g., iqalah not invalidated by invalid conditions, permissibility of reselling to the same buyer before possession) and five issues for the new sale view (e.g., pre-emption applies after iqalah, inability to return for defect if discovered after iqalah). The text also addresses stipulations regarding the price: if the parties stipulate a price different from the original (more, less, or different type), Abu Hanifa says the iqalah stands for the first price and the stipulation is void, while his two disciples have differences (Abu Yusuf sometimes considers it a sale, Muhammad considers stipulating less as a rescission with silence on part of the price). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the school is the view of Muhammad that iqalah is a rescission, as indicated in the text. This passage is crucial for understanding the Hanafi legal construct of iqalah and its nuanced application.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement, linguistically meaning removal and uprooting; in law, it is the removal of the contract.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or rescission of a contract, as opposed to a new contract. In this context, iqalah is a faskh between the parties.
بيع جديد (bay‘ jadīd): A new sale arising from iqalah when considered in the context of third parties (or under certain views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi view, what is the legal nature of iqalah between the two contracting parties?
A) It is always a new sale
B) It is always a rescission (faskh), and if that is impossible, it becomes a new sale
C) It is a rescission for both parties and third parties
D) It is a new sale for both parties and third parties

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, iqalah is primarily a rescission between parties and a new sale for third parties, with the mufta bihi view of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Rulings on Iqlah (Rescission of Sale)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqlah with conditions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of commodity existence for iqlah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on stipulating a different price, and apply the rulings to various scenarios.

Core Idea
Iqlah rescinds a sale only if the commodity exists; a different price condition splits the Hanafi imams.

Iqlah (mutual rescission) is a recognized contract in the Hanafi school. Its validity hinges on the existence of the commodity (mabi‘) at the time of rescission, while the price (thaman) is not similarly required. This lesson unpacks that core condition and then explores the famous intra-Hanafi dispute over what happens when the parties stipulate a price different from the original: does iqlah remain a rescission or become a new sale? The positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad are recorded, with practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its essential condition
Iqlah is valid when the commodity exists; destruction of the commodity prevents it. Note the Arabic phrase: *هلاك المبيع يمنع*.
Destruction of the price does NOT prevent iqlah.
2
Extract the rationale (talil)
The contract is based on the commodity, not the price. The text says: *لأن قيام العقد ليس بالثمن بل بالمعقود عليه*.
This explains why price destruction is irrelevant.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute on stipulating a different price
Abu Hanifa: condition void, iqlah remains a rescission. Abu Yusuf: condition valid, becomes a sale. Muhammad: if more/another type, becomes a sale; if less, remains a rescission.
The dispute stems from differing views on the nature of iqlah.
4
Apply to concrete examples
Work through cases: specific things (valid with compensation if one perishes), muqayada (invalid if either perishes), partial destruction (proportional), special cases (soap drying, land with crop).
Focus on the condition of commodity existence in each.
5
Examine the appendix rulings
Iqlah of iqlah is permissible; iqlah of salam is not; agent's iqlah is not valid by consensus.
These are additional limited cases.

وهلاك الثمن لا يمنع الإقالة، وهلاك المبيع يمنعُ، وهلاك بعضه بقدره. باب الإقالة الأول عند أبي حنيفة، ويلغو ذكر جنس آخر، وعندهما يكون بيعاً، والحاصل أن شرط خلاف الثمن الأول يلغو عند الإمام مطلقاً، وتبقى الإقالة على ما هي عنده من كونها فسخاً. في حق المتعاقدين، وعند أبي يوسف: لا يلغو مطلقاً، وتبقى على ما هو الأصل فيها عنده من حملها على البيع، وعند محمد: يفصل، ففي اشتراط الأكثر وجنس آخر لا يلغو، فتخرج عما هو الأصل فيها عنده من حملها على الفسخ؛ للتعذر، فتحمل على البيع، وفي اشتراط الأقل لا يتعذر حملها على الفسخ؛ لكونه سكوتاً عن بعض الثمن. (عيني، فتح) كما لا يمنع إلخ: لأن قيام العقد ليس بالثمن بل بالمعقود عليه، وهو المبيع دون الثمن لعدم تعينه. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٨/٢] (فتح) يمنع : صحة الإقالة؛ لأن رفع البيع يستدعي قيام البيع؛ إذ رفع المعدوم محال، وقيام البيع بالمبيع دون الثمن؛ لأن الأصل هو المبيع، ولهذا شرط وجوده عند البيع بخلاف الثمن، فإنه بمنزلة الوصف، ولهذا جاز العقد، وإن لم يكن موجوداً كما عرف في الأصول، وتوضيحاته إذا تبايعا عيناً بعين مما يتعين كل واحد منهما للعقد وتقابضا، ثم هلك أحدهما في يد مشتريه، ثم تقايلا، فالإقالة صحيحة، وعلى المشتري قيمة الهلاك إن كان قيمياً، أو مثله إن كان مثلياً يسلمه إلى صاحبه، ويسترد العين منه. وكذا لو تقايلا، والمعقود عليهما قائمان ثم هلك أحدهما، ولو اشترى عيناً مما يتعين للعقد بثمن عين أو دین إذا اشترى عرضاً من العروض بعينه بدراهم أو دنانير، ولا يتعينان في المبادلات، وكذلك الفلوس والكيلي والوزني والعددي؛ لأن أوصافهما ثمن وأعيانهما سلع، ثم تقايلا، فإن كانت العين قائمة في يد المشتري صحت الإقالة، سواء كان الثمن قائماً في يد البائع أو هالكاً؛ لأن الثمن لو كان قائماً فهو في حكم الهالك؛ لأنه لا يتعين للعقد، ولو كان المعقود عليهما عرضين وتبايعا بيع المقايضة وهي بيع العروض بالعروض - ثم هلك العوضان ثم تقايلا، لا تصح الإقالة، وكذلك لو كان أحدهما هالكاً وقت العقد والآخر قائماً، حتى صحت الإقالة، ثم هلك القائم قبل الرد، بطلت الإقالة؛ لأن كلاً منهما مبيع. (عيني، فتح، عناية) وهلاك بعضه [ أي يمنع بقدر الهلاك ويصح في باقيه لقيام المبيع في الباقي، وهذا بالإجماع إلا في رواية للشافعي. (عيني)] لأن الجزء معتبر بالكل، وليس منه ما لو اشترى صابوناً، ثم تقايلا بعد ما نقص وزنه بالجفاف، لا يجب على المشتري شيء؛ لأن كل المبيع باق، ومنه ما لو اشترى أرضاً مع زرعها، وحصدها المشتري ثم تقايلا، صحت في الأرض بحصتها من الثمن، بخلاف ما إذا أدرك الزرع في يده ثم تقايلا فيه فإنها لا تجوز؛ لأن العقد إنما ورد على الفصيل دون الحنطة. تتمة: إقالة الإقالة جائزة، فلو تقايلا البيع ثم تقايلا الإقالة ارتفعت وأعاد البيع، وإقالة السلم لا تقبل الإقالة؛ لكون المسلم فيه دينا سقط، والساقط لا يعود، ولا تجوز إقالة الوكيل بالشراء إجماعاً. (فتح)
And destruction of the price does not prevent iqlah (rescission), and destruction of the commodity prevents it, and destruction of part thereof prevents to its extent. Chapter of Iqlah: The first opinion is of Abu Hanifa, and mentioning another type (of consideration) is void, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it becomes a sale. The summary is that stipulating something other than the first price is void according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) absolutely, and the iqlah remains as it is according to him as being a rescission. With respect to the two contracting parties, according to Abu Yusuf: it is not void absolutely, and it remains according to the principle he holds that it is treated as a sale. According to Muhammad: he differentiates: if stipulating more or another type, it is not void, so it departs from his principle that it is treated as rescission due to impossibility, so it is treated as a sale; and if stipulating less, it is not impossible to treat it as rescission because it is silence regarding part of the price. (Ayni, Fath) 'As it does not prevent' etc.: because the existence of the contract is not by the price but by the object of contract, which is the commodity without the price due to its non-specificity. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 48/2] (Fath) Prevents: the validity of iqlah; because lifting the sale necessitates existence of the sale; since lifting a non-existent is impossible, and existence of the sale is by the commodity without the price; because the origin is the commodity, therefore its existence is required at the time of sale unlike the price, for it is like a description, therefore the contract is permissible even if it was not existing as known in fundamentals. And its clarifications: If two persons barter a specific thing for a specific thing each of which is determined for the contract and they take possession, then one of them perishes in the hand of its buyer, then they rescind (taqayala), the iqlah is valid, and the buyer owes the value of the perished (if it is a qimi item) or its like (if it is mithli) to be delivered to his partner, and he recovers the specific thing from him. Similarly if they rescind while the two objects of contract are existing then one perishes. And if one buys a specific thing determined for the contract for a price which is a specific thing or a debt, if he buys a commodity with its specific (countervalue) in dirhams or dinars which are not determined in exchanges, similarly fulus (copper coins), weighed, measured, and counted items because their characteristics are price and their specific identities are goods, then they rescind: if the specific thing is existing in the hand of the buyer, the iqlah is valid, whether the price is existing in the hand of the seller or perished; because if the price was existing, it is in the ruling of perished because it is not determined for the contract. And if the two objects of contract were commodities and they bartered (bay' al-muqayada) which is sale of goods for goods, then both considerations perished then they rescind, the iqlah is not valid. Similarly if one of them was perished at time of contract and the other existing, even if the iqlah was valid then the existing perished before return, the iqlah becomes void; because each is a commodity. (Ayni, Fath, Inaya) 'And destruction of part' [i.e. prevents to the extent of the destruction and is valid regarding the remainder due to existence of the commodity in the remainder, and this by consensus except in a transmission from al-Shafi'i. (Ayni)] because the part is considered like the whole. And it does not include the case where one bought soap and then they rescind after its weight decreased by drying: nothing is due from the buyer because all the commodity remains. And from it is the case where one bought land with its crop and the buyer harvested it then they rescind: it is valid regarding the land with its share of the price, contrary to the case where the crop ripened in his hand then they rescind regarding it: it is not permissible; because the contract only applied to the unripe grain (fasil) not the wheat. Appendix: Iqlah of iqlah is permissible: if they rescind the sale then rescind the iqlah, the sale is restored and renewed. Iqlah of salam (advance payment) is not accepted because the subject matter is a debt that has fallen, and the fallen does not return. And iqlah of an agent for purchase is not permissible by consensus. (Fath)
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature and conditions of iqlah (rescission of sale), with extensive commentary from 'Ayni and Fath. The core ruling is that destruction of the commodity prevents iqlah, but destruction of the price does not, because the contract subsists through the commodity not the price. The key dispute among the Hanafi imams is about the effect of stipulating a condition different from the original price when rescinding: Abu Hanifa holds such a condition void and maintains the iqlah as a rescission; Abu Yusuf considers it always a sale; Muhammad differentiates based on whether the condition is for more or less. The text then elaborates with detailed examples: if the parties exchange specific items and one perishes, iqlah is valid with compensation; if both are perishable goods (muqayada), iqlah is invalid if either perishes. Partial destruction prevents iqlah proportionally. Special cases include soap drying (no compensation), land with crop (valid for land only), and the appendix on iqlah of iqlah being permissible, but not for salam or agent's purchase.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqlah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement of the parties, returning the commodity to the seller and the price to the buyer.
مثلي (mithli): An item that has a standard like-kind substitute in the market (e.g., dirhams, dinars, measured or weighed goods).
قيمي (qimi): An item that has no standard substitute in the market; compensation is by market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if two parties rescind a sale (iqlah) and the commodity has partially perished?
A) The iqlah is completely void because the commodity is partially destroyed.
B) The iqlah is valid only for the remaining part, with a proportional reduction in the price.
C) The iqlah is valid for the entire commodity, and the buyer compensates the seller for the destroyed part.
D) The iqlah is invalid unless the destroyed part is replaced.

Key Takeaway
Iqlah is valid only if the commodity exists; intra-Hanafi dispute exists on the effect of different price conditions.

Definitions and Types of Sale: Tawliyah and Murabahah
Defines tawliyah and murabahah and lists types of sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of tawliyah and murabahah, distinguish them from iqalah, and list the five types of sale based on consideration.

Core Idea
Tawliyah is sale at cost; Murabahah is cost plus profit; both require a prior known price.

This lesson introduces two important sale contracts in Hanafi fiqh: tawliyah (sale at cost) and murabahah (cost-plus sale). The text also clarifies how these differ from iqalah (rescission) and presents a refined definition that avoids the limitation of prior ownership. Understanding these definitions is foundational before exploring conditions and rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central definitions
Read the definitions of tawliyah and murabahah as given by the author. Note that tawliyah is at the first price without increase or decrease, while murabahah includes an additional profit.
The author refines al-Quduri's definition to include cases where the seller did not physically own the item by the first contract.
2
Distinguish tawliyah from iqalah
Understand the distinction: iqalah is a mutual cancellation with the original seller at the original price, while tawliyah can be to a third party. If the buyer sells back to the original seller using the word 'sale', it is a sale, not iqalah.
Iqalah is a revocable rescission; tawliyah is a new sale.
3
Learn the five types of sale
Memorize the five types of sale based on consideration: musawamah (negotiated price), murabahah (cost+profit), tawliyah (cost), ishtirak (partnership), and wadi'ah (discount).
These are all valid forms of sale in the Hanafi school, each with distinct conditions.
4
Analyze the proof from Sunnah
The hadith of Abu Bakr and the Prophet shows that tawliyah was practiced. The Prophet asked to be made wali of one camel and refused it without price, indicating that tawliyah is a valid transfer at cost.
The dalil is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and the inference is zanni al-dalalah, yet sufficient for permissibility.

الزيادة في التعليم هو ذكاؤه، والأولى أن يعلل بعدم العرف حتى لو كان ثمه عرف ظاهر ضم. (عيني، فتح) وكراء: لعدم العرف بإلحاقه رأس المال.
Chapter of Tawliyah and Murabahah. Explanation of their rulings. A sale at a previous price, and Murabahah with it and with an increase. Their condition is that the first price be mithli. In the Shari'ah, it is a sale at a previous price with an increase of profit, i.e., the condition of Tawliyah and Murabahah is that the fuller's fee be added to the capital. The chapter on Tawliyah and Murabahah and the connection between the two chapters: that Iqalah is transferring the sold item back to the seller at the same first price, and Tawliyah is also a transfer, but mostly to someone other than the seller. Even if the buyer sells the item to the seller at its first price, it is permissible and it is a sale, not an Iqalah, because being an Iqalah is conditional on not using the word 'sale'. All this requires a prior contract. Iqalah is to Tawliyah as the singular is to the compound, because Iqalah only occurs with the seller, while Tawliyah is more general, occurring with the seller and others. (Miskin, Fath) It is a sale, etc., i.e., at the first price without increase or decrease. ('Ayni) This definition is better than that of al-Quduri, who said: 'Tawliyah and Murabahah are the transfer of what one owned by the first contract at the first price without increase or with an increase of profit,' because in this contract it is not required to transfer what one owned by the first contract; even if the usurped property is lost with the usurper and he guarantees its value, then finds it, it is permissible for him to sell it as Murabahah and Tawliyah on the basis of what he guaranteed, even if it does not involve transferring what he owned by the first contract. The customary, half. Then know that with respect to the consideration, sale is divided into five types: first: Musawamah, which is a sale at any price agreed upon; second: Murabahah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price with an increase of profit; third: Tawliyah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price without increase or decrease; fourth: Ishtirak, which is a sale of Tawliyah or Murabahah in part of the sold item and the like; fifth: Wadi'ah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price with a slight decrease. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 49/2) It is called Tawliyah because the seller makes the buyer a wali (guardian) over what he bought with his purchase. Its proof from the Sunnah is what is narrated that Abu Bakr bought two camels, and he (the Prophet) said to him: 'Make me the wali of one of them.' He said: 'It is for you without anything.' He said: 'As for without a price, no.' (Fath) The first price, etc., i.e., that which the buyer owns by that price, the sold item is obligatory by the contract. ('Ayni) Like measured and weighed items. If it is not mithli, such as a slave or a garment, then Murabahah and Tawliyah are not realized, because if it is not mithli, its amount is not known. However, if he sells it with that consideration to someone who owns it, or with it and a known profit increase, then it is permissible, because ignorance is removed. If he sells it with it and with a tenth of its value or price, it is not permissible, because he sold it with that and a part of that unknown consideration. If the consideration is mithli, and he sells it with it and a tenth of it, if the buyer knows the total price, it is valid; otherwise, if he knows in the session, it is permissible and he has the option; otherwise, it is corrupt. One of its conditions is that it not be a currency exchange (sarf), so if he sells dinars for dirhams, Tawliyah and Murabahah are not permissible. ('Ayni, Fath) Mithli means that which has a likeness, such as dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, and countable items with close sizes. (T, 'Ayni) That he add to the capital the fuller's fee, and likewise the fee for washing, sewing, the expense of plastering the house and plastering it, digging the well, digging the canals, irrigating crops and trees. The dyeing, embroidery, twisting, carrying of food, driving of sheep, and he says: it cost me such-and-such, i.e., and the fee for driving, etc. And he does not include the fee for the shepherd, education, and the rent of the storage house. Then if the seller cheats. The dye (sibgh) with fatha is the source, and with kasra is the material with which one dyes. The general rule for inclusion is that everything that increases the sold item or its value is included. 'Ayni and others relied on the custom of merchants for inclusion. If he does any of the mentioned things with his own hand, it is not included. The food, clothing, and rent of the sold item are included, and the fee of the simsar if it was stipulated in the contract. The fee of the dallal is not included by consensus. The simsar is the one who indicates the location of the commodity or its owner, and the dallal is the one accompanying the commodity. In al-Nihayah: the simsar is from the buyer's side, and the dallal from the seller's side. ('Ayni, Fath) And the embroidery, i.e., the fee for the embroidery, i.e., the mark of the garment; and the twisting, i.e., the fee for twisting, from 'I twisted the garment' when I made its edge, as is done on the edges of scarves and shawls. ('Ayni) And carrying the food, i.e., the fee for carrying the food, i.e., moving it from the place of purchase. ('Ayni) And he says, i.e., the seller in Tawliyah and Murabahah, who is the first buyer. ('Ayni) 'for such-and-such' meaning dirhams or dinars, and he should not say 'I bought it for such-and-such' to avoid lying. ('Ayni) And he does not include, etc. Similarly, he does not include the fee of the doctor, the trainer, the veterinarian, the returned runaway slave, the circumcision fee, the fines in torts, what is taken on the road from oppression, unless it is customary to include it. If he marries off his slave, he does not include the dower in the capital. Nor does he include what he spent on himself during his journey of clothing, food, mount, oil, and laundry. ('Ayni, Fath) And education: i.e., he does not include what he spent on the slave for teaching a craft, Quran, poetry, or anything else, because the increase from education is his intelligence. It is better to explain it by the lack of custom, even if there is an apparent custom, it is included. ('Ayni, Fath) And rent: due to lack of custom to annex it to the capital.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the contracts of tawliyah (sale at cost) and murabahah (sale with profit). The author begins by defining these two types of sale, stating that tawliyah is a sale at the original price (the price paid by the seller), while murabahah is a sale at the original price plus a profit. A key condition for both is that the first price (the seller's cost) must be mithli—fungible goods like dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or countable items. If the goods are not mithli (e.g., a unique slave or garment), the seller cannot claim the exact cost, leading to uncertainty (gharar). The text distinguishes tawliyah from iqalah (rescission), noting that iqalah is specific to the original seller, while tawliyah can be to a third party. A better definition than al-Quduri's is given, emphasizing that the seller need not have physically owned the good by the first contract (e.g., a guarantor selling a lost and found item). The passage then lists the five types of sale based on consideration: musawamah (bargaining), murabahah (cost plus profit), tawliyah (cost), ishtirak (partnership sale), and wadi'ah (discount). The proof from Sunnah is the hadith of Abu Bakr and the Prophet, demonstrating the permissibility of tawliyah. The text then details that the sale must not be a currency exchange (sarf), as that would void tawliyah and murabahah. A significant portion explains what expenses can be added to the principal: costs that increase the value or utility of the good, such as fuller's fees, dyeing, sewing, transporting food, etc., but not personal expenses like the seller's travel costs, or training fees unless customary. The general rule is that anything that increases the good or its value is included, but if done personally, it is not. The commentary emphasizes that custom plays a role in determining inclusion.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership of an item to the buyer at the exact price the seller paid for it, without any profit or loss.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller sells an item at the price he originally paid plus an agreed profit margin.
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement, transferring the sold item back to the seller at the original price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how many types of sale are there based on consideration (badal)?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Tawliyah and murabahah are valid sale contracts that require disclosure of the original cost, and the Hanafi definition does not require prior ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Condition of Mithli and Exclusion of Sarf in Tawliyah and Murabahah
Specifies condition of mithli and exclusion of sarf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first price must be mithli and why a currency exchange (sarf) invalidates tawliyah and murabahah.

Core Idea
Tawliyah and murabahah require a mithli price to avoid uncertainty; sarf is excluded because it involves immediate exchange of same type.

This lesson focuses on two critical conditions for tawliyah and murabahah: the first price must be mithli (fungible) and the transaction cannot be a sarf (currency exchange). The text explains that if the price is not mithli, uncertainty arises, and if it is sarf, the profit concept is incompatible. Understanding these conditions prevents invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of mithli
Read the definition of mithli: goods that have exact equivalents, like dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or countable items. The first price must be of this type to know the exact cost.
If the price is not mithli (e.g., a unique slave), the cost cannot be determined precisely, leading to gharar.
2
Apply the condition to cases
If the price is mithli and the seller sells it with a tenth of its value, it is valid only if the buyer knows the total. If not mithli, it is invalid unless sold to someone who owns the price or with a known profit.
The text gives examples: selling a non-mithli item at cost plus a fraction of its value is invalid because the fraction is unknown.
3
Learn the exclusion of sarf
Sarf is the exchange of gold for silver (or currency). Tawliyah and murabahah are not valid in sarf because the profit or cost concept does not apply; the exchange must be immediate and equal? Actually, the text simply says it is not permissible.
The reason is that sarf requires equality and hand-to-hand transaction, and adding profit or cost would violate these rules.
4
Summarize the rulings
Consolidate: Tawliyah and murabahah are valid only when the price is mithli and the transaction is not sarf. If the price is non-mithli, the contract can be salvaged if the buyer knows the cost or if it is sold to the owner of the price.
These conditions ensure the contract is free from gharar.

Chapter of Tawliyah and Murabahah. Explanation of their rulings. A sale at a previous price, and Murabahah with it and with an increase. Their condition is that the first price be mithli. In the Shari'ah, it is a sale at a previous price with an increase of profit, i.e., the condition of Tawliyah and Murabahah is that the fuller's fee be added to the capital. The chapter on Tawliyah and Murabahah and the connection between the two chapters: that Iqalah is transferring the sold item back to the seller at the same first price, and Tawliyah is also a transfer, but mostly to someone other than the seller. Even if the buyer sells the item to the seller at its first price, it is permissible and it is a sale, not an Iqalah, because being an Iqalah is conditional on not using the word 'sale'. All this requires a prior contract. Iqalah is to Tawliyah as the singular is to the compound, because Iqalah only occurs with the seller, while Tawliyah is more general, occurring with the seller and others. Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the contracts of tawliyah (sale at cost) and murabahah (sale with profit). The author begins by defining these two types of sale, stating that tawliyah is a sale at the original price (the price paid by the seller), while murabahah is a sale at the original price plus a profit. A better definition than al-Quduri's is given, emphasizing that the seller need not have physically owned the good by the first contract (e.g., a guarantor selling a lost and found item). The general rule is that anything that increases the good or its value is included, but if done personally, it is not. The commentary emphasizes that custom plays a role in determining inclusion.

Key Terms
مثلي (mithli): Goods that have exact equivalents in the market, such as dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or countable items with close dimensions.
صرف (sarf): Exchange of gold for silver or currency exchange, which requires immediate hand-to-hand transaction and equality of quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person sells a mithli item (like wheat) at tawliyah for a non-mithli price (like a unique vase), is the tawliyah valid?
A) Yes, because the sold item is mithli.
B) No, because the price is not mithli.
C) Yes, if the profit is known.
D) No, because it is a sarf.

Key Takeaway
Tawliyah and murabahah require the first price to be mithli and the transaction must not be a currency exchange.

Expenses That Can Be Added to Capital in Tawliyah and Murabahah
Details expenses that can be added to capital.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general rule for which expenses can be added to the capital in tawliyah and murabahah, and identify exceptions.

Core Idea
Expenses that increase the value or utility of the good are added to capital; personal expenses and customary fees follow custom.

This lesson details the extensive list of expenses that a seller may include in the capital when selling by tawliyah or murabahah. The text provides a general rule: anything that increases the item or its value is included, but there are many exceptions based on custom and type of expense. Understanding this ensures correct pricing and avoids disputes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
The text states: 'the general rule for inclusion is that everything that increases the sold item or its value is included.' Identify this rule and its source.
This rule is affirmed by 'Ayni and others, relying on custom.
2
List the expenses that are added
Read the list: fuller's fee, washing, sewing, plastering, well-digging, canal-digging, irrigation, dyeing, embroidery, twisting, carrying food, driving sheep, etc.
These are costs that directly improve or transport the goods.
3
Identify expenses not added
Excluded expenses: fee of shepherd, education, rent of storage, doctor, trainer, veterinarian, runaway slave recovery, circumcision, tort penalties, road oppression fees, and personal travel expenses.
The reason sometimes is lack of custom or that benefit is not directly on the good.
4
Apply the role of custom
Custom can override the default rules. For example, if it is customary to include education fees, they are included. The text emphasizes custom as a decisive factor.
This shows the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh based on 'urf.

Chapter of Tawliyah and Murabahah. Explanation of their rulings. A sale at a previous price, and Murabahah with it and with an increase. Their condition is that the first price be mithli. In the Shari'ah, it is a sale at a previous price with an increase of profit, i.e., the condition of Tawliyah and Murabahah is that the fuller's fee be added to the capital. The chapter on Tawliyah and Murabahah and the connection between the two chapters: that Iqalah is transferring the sold item back to the seller at the same first price, and Tawliyah is also a transfer, but mostly to someone other than the seller. Even if the buyer sells the item to the seller at its first price, it is permissible and it is a sale, not an Iqalah, because being an Iqalah is conditional on not using the word 'sale'. All this requires a prior contract. Iqalah is to Tawliyah as the singular is to the compound, because Iqalah only occurs with the seller, while Tawliyah is more general, occurring with the seller and others. (Miskin, Fath) It is a sale, etc., i.e., at the first price without increase or decrease. ('Ayni) This definition is better than that of al-Quduri, who said: 'Tawliyah and Murabahah are the transfer of what one owned by the first contract at the first price without increase or with an increase of profit,' because in this contract it is not required to transfer what one owned by the first contract; even if the usurped property is lost with the usurper and he guarantees its value, then finds it, it is permissible for him to sell it as Murabahah and Tawliyah on the basis of what he guaranteed, even if it does not involve transferring what he owned by the first contract. The customary, half. Then know that with respect to the consideration, sale is divided into five types: first: Musawamah, which is a sale at any price agreed upon; second: Murabahah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price with an increase of profit; third: Tawliyah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price without increase or decrease; fourth: Ishtirak, which is a sale of Tawliyah or Murabahah in part of the sold item and the like; fifth: Wadi'ah, which is transferring ownership of the sold item at the first price with a slight decrease. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 49/2) It is called Tawliyah because the seller makes the buyer a wali (guardian) over what he bought with his purchase. Its proof from the Sunnah is what is narrated that Abu Bakr bought two camels, and he (the Prophet) said to him: 'Make me the wali of one of them.' He said: 'It is for you without anything.' He said: 'As for without a price, no.' (Fath) The first price, etc., i.e., that which the buyer owns by that price, the sold item is obligatory by the contract. ('Ayni) Like measured and weighed items. If it is not mithli, such as a slave or a garment, then Murabahah and Tawliyah are not realized, because if it is not mithli, its amount is not known. However, if he sells it with that consideration to someone who owns it, or with it and a known profit increase, then it is permissible, because ignorance is removed. If he sells it with it and with a tenth of its value or price, it is not permissible, because he sold it with that and a part of that unknown consideration. If the consideration is mithli, and he sells it with it and a tenth of it, if the buyer knows the total price, it is valid; otherwise, if he knows in the session, it is permissible and he has the option; otherwise, it is corrupt. One of its conditions is that it not be a currency exchange (sarf), so if he sells dinars for dirhams, Tawliyah and Murabahah are not permissible. ('Ayni, Fath) Mithli means that which has a likeness, such as dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, and countable items with close sizes. (T, 'Ayni) That he add to the capital the fuller's fee, and likewise the fee for washing, sewing, the expense of plastering the house and plastering it, digging the well, digging the canals, irrigating crops and trees. The dyeing, embroidery, twisting, carrying of food, driving of sheep, and he says: it cost me such-and-such, i.e., and the fee for driving, etc. And he does not include the fee for the shepherd, education, and the rent of the storage house. Then if the seller cheats. The dye (sibgh) with fatha is the source, and with kasra is the material with which one dyes. The general rule for inclusion is that everything that increases the sold item or its value is included. 'Ayni and others relied on the custom of merchants for inclusion. If he does any of the mentioned things with his own hand, it is not included. The food, clothing, and rent of the sold item are included, and the fee of the simsar if it was stipulated in the contract. The fee of the dallal is not included by consensus. The simsar is the one who indicates the location of the commodity or its owner, and the dallal is the one accompanying the commodity. In al-Nihayah: the simsar is from the buyer's side, and the dallal from the seller's side. ('Ayni, Fath) And the embroidery, i.e., the fee for the embroidery, i.e., the mark of the garment; and the twisting, i.e., the fee for twisting, from 'I twisted the garment' when I made its edge, as is done on the edges of scarves and shawls. ('Ayni) And carrying the food, i.e., the fee for carrying the food, i.e., moving it from the place of purchase. ('Ayni) And he says, i.e., the seller in Tawliyah and Murabahah, who is the first buyer. ('Ayni) 'for such-and-such' meaning dirhams or dinars, and he should not say 'I bought it for such-and-such' to avoid lying. ('Ayni) And he does not include, etc. Similarly, he does not include the fee of the doctor, the trainer, the veterinarian, the returned runaway slave, the circumcision fee, the fines in torts, what is taken on the road from oppression, unless it is customary to include it. If he marries off his slave, he does not include the dower in the capital. Nor does he include what he spent on himself during his journey of clothing, food, mount, oil, and laundry. ('Ayni, Fath) And education: i.e., he does not include what he spent on the slave for teaching a craft, Quran, poetry, or anything else, because the increase from education is his intelligence. It is better to explain it by the lack of custom, even if there is an apparent custom, it is included. ('Ayni, Fath) And rent: due to lack of custom to annex it to the capital.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the contracts of tawliyah (sale at cost) and murabahah (sale with profit). The author begins by defining these two types of sale, stating that tawliyah is a sale at the original price (the price paid by the seller), while murabahah is a sale at the original price plus a profit. A key condition for both is that the first price (the seller's cost) must be mithli—fungible goods like dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or countable items. If the goods are not mithli (e.g., a unique slave or garment), the seller cannot claim the exact cost, leading to uncertainty (gharar). The text distinguishes tawliyah from iqalah (rescission), noting that iqalah is specific to the original seller, while tawliyah can be to a third party. A better definition than al-Quduri's is given, emphasizing that the seller need not have physically owned the good by the first contract (e.g., a guarantor selling a lost and found item). The passage then lists the five types of sale based on consideration: musawamah (bargaining), murabahah (cost plus profit), tawliyah (cost), ishtirak (partnership sale), and wadi'ah (discount). The proof from Sunnah is the hadith of Abu Bakr and the Prophet, demonstrating the permissibility of tawliyah. The text then details that the sale must not be a currency exchange (sarf), as that would void tawliyah and murabahah. A significant portion explains what expenses can be added to the principal: costs that increase the value or utility of the good, such as fuller's fees, dyeing, sewing, transporting food, etc., but not personal expenses like the seller's travel costs, or training fees unless customary. The general rule is that anything that increases the good or its value is included, but if done personally, it is not. The commentary emphasizes that custom plays a role in determining inclusion.

Key Terms
السمسار (simsar): A broker who indicates the location of the commodity or its owner, usually from the buyer's side.
الدلال (dallal): An agent who accompanies the commodity, usually from the seller's side.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): Capital or the original cost price of the goods, which may include certain expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expenses can be added to the capital in a murabahah sale according to the text?
A) Seller's own food during travel
B) Fee for training a slave
C) Fuller's fee
D) Rent of storage house

Key Takeaway
Only expenses that increase the value or utility of the sold item, as per custom, may be added to the capital; personal costs and those not adding direct value are excluded.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Remedies for Fraud (Khiyanah) in Tawliyah and Murabahah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Presents the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on khiyānah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on khiyanah in tawliyah and murabahah, explain the reasoning behind each, and determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each opinion.

Core Idea
When fraud occurs in a trust-based sale, the buyer may have a choice (khiyar) or a right to price reduction, depending on the Hanafi authority.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khiyanah (fraud) in two types of trust-based contracts: tawliyah (sale at cost price) and murabahah (cost-plus-profit). The source text from a Hanafi manual presents the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with references to other schools. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school balances individual rights, contractual integrity, and market fairness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The seller declares a false original price (e.g., says he bought for 10 when it was 9) and sells on tawliyah or murabahah. Recognize the two transaction types and the element of misrepresentation.
Tawliyah involves no profit; murabahah involves a declared profit margin.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
In both contracts, the buyer has khiyar (option to cancel or accept at the true price). The sale is built on the first price; misrepresentation triggers a choice to affirm or rescind.
Abu Hanifa treats the false statement as a defect in the contractual basis.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and its basis
Automatic reduction: in tawliyah, deduct the fraud amount from the price; in murabahah, reduce profit proportionally. The sale is on the first price, so it must be adjusted to reflect reality.
Abu Yusuf sees the remedy as an automatic correction, not a unilateral choice.
4
State Muhammad's position and its basis
Buyer has option in both because mentioning the profit or cost is a description; when false, the buyer has khiyar (similar to misdescription in contracts). This is analogous to defects in goods.
Muhammad emphasizes the promissory nature of the description as an inducement.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the buyer can cancel the sale; under Abu Yusuf, the price is forced lower. Also note other schools' views (some hold sale invalid). The mufta bihi is not specified in this passage.
This difference affects whether the buyer retains the option to walk away.

Source Text

فإن خان إلخ إلى قوله: "حط في التولية"، صورته: اشترى شيئاً بتسعة، وقال لآخر: اشتريته بعشرة وأبيعك بربح درهم فاشتراه على ذلك ثم تبين أنه خانه في درهم، يخير عند أبي حنيفة إن شاء أخذ المبيع بكل الثمن وإن شاء ترك، ولو خان في بيع التولية على هذا الوجه يحط قدر الخيانة عنده، وعند أبي يوسف يحط قدر الخيانة في التولية ويحط قدر ما يقابلها من الربح في المرابحة، وبه قال أحمد والشافعي في قول؛ لأن بيع التولية والمرابحة على الثمن الأول فيقدر بقدره، غير أنه في التولية يحط قدر الخيانة وفي المرابحة من الربح أيضاً بحسابه؛ لأن الربح ينقسم على رأس المال وعلى قدر الخيانة. وعند محمد: يخير فيهما جميعاً؛ لأنهما باشرا عقدا باختيارهما بثمن معلوم فينعقد بالمسمى؛ لأنهما تراضيا على ذلك، وذكر المرابحة والتولية للترويج، والترغيب تجري مجرى الوصف، فإذا فات الوصف المرغوب فيه يتخير كما في سائر الأوصاف، و به قال مالك وأحمد والشافعي في قول، وعن مالك وهو قول من الشافعي: لا يصح البيع، وللإمام: أنه لو لم يحط في التولية لا يبقى تولية؛ لأنها تكون بالثمن الأول، وهذا يزيد عليه فتغير التصرف فتعين الحط،
If he cheats, etc., up to his saying: 'He reduces in tawliyah'. The scenario: He bought something for nine, and said to another: 'I bought it for ten and I sell it to you with a profit of one dirham.' So the other bought it on that basis, then it became clear that he cheated him by one dirham. According to Abu Hanifa, he has the option: if he wishes, he may take the sold item at the full price, or if he wishes, he may leave it. If he cheated in a tawliyah sale in this manner, according to him (Abu Hanifa) he must reduce the amount of the fraud. According to Abu Yusuf, he reduces the amount of the fraud in tawliyah, and in murabahah he reduces the amount corresponding to it from the profit. Ahmad and al-Shafi'i in one opinion said likewise, because the sale of tawliyah and murabahah is based on the first price, so it must be according to it. However, in tawliyah the amount of fraud is reduced, and in murabahah from the profit as well proportionally, because the profit is divided between the capital and the amount of fraud. According to Muhammad, he has the option in both, because they entered into a contract by their choice at a known price, so it is concluded at the specified amount, because they mutually agreed upon that. Mentioning murabahah and tawliyah is for promotion, and inducement acts as a description. When the desired description is missing, he has the option, just as in other descriptions. This is also the opinion of Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion. From Malik and also one saying of al-Shafi'i: the sale is not valid. And according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), that if he does not reduce in tawliyah, it would no longer be tawliyah, because it is at the first price, and this exceeds it, so the transaction changes, so reduction is determined.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a seller in a tawliyah or murabahah contract falsely claims the original purchase price, thereby cheating the buyer. The text presents the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and also mentions the views of other schools. The case is a buyer who says he bought an item for ten when he actually bought it for nine, and sells it with a profit of one dirham (so the claimed price is ten plus one profit = eleven). When the truth emerges, Abu Hanifa holds that in tawliyah (where no profit is added), the buyer is given a choice: either accept the item at the true price (nine) or cancel the sale. In murabahah, the same choice applies. Abu Yusuf says that in tawliyah, the amount of fraud is simply deducted from the price, while in murabahah, the deduction is proportional: the profit is divided between the capital and the fraudulent increase. Muhammad says that in both cases, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either accept at the declared price or cancel, because the mention of the profit or cost is a description, and if that description is false, the buyer has a choice. The text then notes that Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i support the view of Abu Hanifa (or Abu Yusuf) in one narration, and in another they hold the sale invalid. The passage concludes with a reasoning from the Imam (likely Abu Hanifa) that in tawliyah, if the fraud is not corrected by reduction, the sale would no longer be tawliyah since it exceeds the first price. This lesson highlights the interplay between textual conditions and the principle of fair dealing in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyānah): Cheating or fraud in declaring the original purchase price in a tawliyah or murabahah transaction.
حط (hatt): The reduction or deduction from the price to correct the fraud, either by reducing the principal (in tawliyah) or proportionally from profit (in murabahah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what remedy does a buyer have if a seller cheats on the declared cost in a tawliyah sale?
A) The buyer must accept the sale at the true cost with no option
B) The buyer has the option to cancel the sale or accept at the true price
C) The seller pays a penalty of double the fraud amount
D) The sale is automatically void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fraud in a trust-based sale does not automatically invalidate the contract; the buyer may have the right to cancel or demand a price adjustment, depending on which Imam's view is followed.
