Expenses Added to Cost Price in Tawliyah and Murabahah
Lists expenses to include/exclude from capital in fiduciary sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for including expenses in the cost price of a fiduciary sale and correctly classify given expenses as included or excluded according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
In fiduciary sales, only expenses that increase the item's value or are customary are added to cost; personal and maintenance costs are not.

Fiduciary sales like tawliyah and murabahah rely on the seller's honest disclosure of the cost price. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary from al-Ayni and others, provides a detailed list of which expenses may be included in that cost. Understanding this list is crucial for both merchants and students of fiqh to ensure transactions are free from deception and ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the sale type
Determine whether the transaction is tawliyah (sale at cost) or murabahah (sale with stated profit). Both require accurate disclosure of capital (thaman).
The same rules for expense inclusion apply to both.
2
2. Apply the general rule
Recall the Hanafi principle: any expense that increases the item itself or its market value is included; expenses that merely maintain the item or benefit the seller personally are excluded, unless custom dictates otherwise.
Custom (urf) is a key determinant; the text relies on 'ādah al-tujjār.
3
3. Classify each expense from the passage
Go through the listed expenses (e.g., dyeing, carrying, storage rent, broker fees, teaching, travel) and classify each as included or excluded based on the rule and the text's explicit rulings.
The passage provides specific examples; note the distinction between simsar (included if stipulated) and dallāl (never included by consensus).
4
4. Note the exception for seller's own labor
If the seller performs the work himself (e.g., dyes the cloth), no expense is added, because no actual outlay occurred.
This prevents double-counting or imaginary costs.
5
5. Understand the role of custom
Realize that many rulings depend on the prevalent custom of merchants. For teaching a slave or renting storage, inclusion hinges on whether custom treats them as part of the cost.
This reflects the Hanafi reliance on urf as a subsidiary source.

Chapter of Tawliyah and Murabahah. And dyeing, embroidery, twisting, carrying food, and driving sheep, and he says: it cost me such-and-such, i.e., the wage of driving etc., and the wage of the shepherd, teaching, and rent of the storage house are not included. If the seller is treacherous... Dyeing: with fatha it is the source, with kasra what is dyed with. The rule for inclusion is that anything that increases the item or its value is included, and al-Ayni and others relied on the custom of merchants to include it. If he does any of the mentioned things himself, it is not included. The food of the item, its covering, and its rent are included, and the fee of the broker if stipulated in the contract. The fee of the dallal is not included by consensus. The simsar is the one who indicates the place of the commodity or its owner, and the dallal is the one accompanying the commodity. In an-Nihaya: the simsar is from the buyer's side, the dallal from the seller's side. (Ayni, Fath) And at-tiraz: i.e., the wage of tiraz, i.e., the embroidery of the cloth. And al-fatl: i.e., the wage of twisting, from 'I twisted the cloth' when you produce its fringe, as done at the edges of handkerchiefs and shawls. (Ayni) And carrying the food: i.e., the wage of carrying the food, i.e., its transfer from the place of purchase. (Ayni) And he says: i.e., the seller in tawliyah and murabahah, and he is the first buyer. (Ayni) 'with such-and-such': from dirhams or dinars, and he should not say 'I bought it for such-and-such' to avoid lying. (Ayni) And it is not included, etc.: similarly, the wage of the doctor, trainer, and veterinarian, the recovery of a runaway slave, the wage of circumcision, minor crimes, what is taken on the road from zulm, unless custom includes it, and if he married off his slave, the dowry is not added to the capital. And what he spent on himself during his travel from clothing, food, mount, oil, and washing clothes is not included. (Ayni, Fath) And teaching: i.e., what is spent on the slave for teaching a craft, Quran, poetry, or anything else is not included, because the increase due to teaching is his intelligence, but it is better to attribute it to lack of custom, unless there is a clear custom, then it is included. (Ayni, Fath) And rent: due to lack of custom to include it in the capital.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage, from the chapter of Tawliyah and Murabahah, provides a detailed list of expenses that may or may not be added to the capital (thaman) when stating the cost price in fiduciary sales. The general rule given by al-Ayni and others is that anything that increases the item itself or its value is included; if it only maintains the item or is for personal benefit, it is not. The text distinguishes between wages for services directly related to the item (like carrying, feeding, and renting storage) which are included if customary, and personal expenses (like the seller's own travel costs) which are not. Notably, the fee of a samsar (broker who locates the item) is included if stipulated, while the fee of a dallal (one who accompanies the item) is never included by consensus. Teaching a slave is not included because the benefit is in the slave's intelligence, not a direct cost, but custom can override. The reasoning relies on urf (custom) and the principle that capital does not cover speculative increases.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): Selling at the same price as the seller paid, without profit.
مرابحة (murabahah): Selling with a stated profit percentage on the cost price.
ضم (damm): Adding expenses to the capital (thaman) in fiduciary sales.
سمسار (simsar): A broker who indicates the location of the commodity or its owner.
دلال (dallal): A broker who accompanies the commodity (middleman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The seller's travel expenses for food and clothing
B) The fee of a dallal (accompanying broker) by consensus
C) The rent of a storage house if customary
D) The cost of teaching a slave a craft

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiduciary sales, only expenses that increase the item's value or are customary are added to the cost price; personal and maintenance expenses are not.

Three Hanafi Remedies for Treachery in Fiduciary Sales
Intra-Hanafi dispute on price fraud remedy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the buyer's remedy when a seller misrepresents the cost price in tawliyah and murabahah, and apply the mnemonic 'fatar' to identify each imam's view.

Core Idea
Three Hanafi imams give different remedies for price fraud in fiduciary sales: option, deduction, or option in both.

When a seller in a trust-based sale (tawliyah or murabahah) lies about the original cost, the buyer needs a remedy. The Hanafi school has three distinct opinions from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. This lesson unpacks each view, the underlying reasoning, and the practical difference. You will also learn a clever mnemonic to remember which imam said what.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The seller buys for 9, claims it cost 10, and sells with 1 dirham profit (total 11). The actual cost is 9, so he cheated by 1. Identify the contract type: tawliyah (cost plus no profit) or murabahah (cost plus stated profit).
In tawliyah, the buyer pays exactly the cost; in murabahah, cost plus profit.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
In murabahah, the buyer may either take the item at the falsely stated price (10) or cancel. In tawliyah, the buyer deducts the cheated amount (1) from the price.
Reason: tawliyah is directly tied to the first price; murabahah has a profit element.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's position
In both tawliyah and murabahah, the buyer deducts the cheated amount. In murabahah, the profit is reduced proportionally.
Profit is divided between the true capital and the cheated amount.
4
Identify Muhammad's position
In both contracts, the buyer has an option: take at stated price or cancel. The profit disclosure is treated as a mere description.
Mentioning murabahah/tawliyah is just for promotion, like a warranty.
5
Learn the mnemonic 'fatar'
فطر: فـ = تفصيل (Abu Hanifa), طـ = حط (Abu Yusuf), رـ = تخيير (Muhammad). Use it to recall who said what.
Also note: if the item perishes before return, the option drops and full price is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن خان إلخ إلى قوله: "حط في التولية"، صورته: اشترى شيئاً بتسعة، وقال لآخر: اشتريته بعشرة وأبيعك بربح درهم فاشتراه على ذلك ثم تبين أنه خانه في درهم، يخير عند أبي حنيفة إن شاء أخذ المبيع بكل الثمن وإن شاء ترك، ولو خان في بيع التولية على هذا الوجه يحط قدر الخيانة عنده، وعند أبي يوسف يحط قدر الخيانة في التولية ويحط قدر ما يقابلها من الربح في المرابحة، وبه قال أحمد والشافعي في قول؛ لأن بيع التولية والمرابحة على الثمن الأول فيقدر بقدره، غير أنه في التولية يحط قدر الخيانة وفي المرابحة من الربح أيضاً بحسابه؛ لأن الربح ينقسم على رأس المال وعلى قدر الخيانة. وعند محمد: يخير فيهما جميعاً؛ لأنهما باشرا عقدا باختيارهما بثمن معلوم فينعقد بالمسمى؛ لأنهما تراضيا على ذلك، وذكر المرابحة والتولية للترويج، والترغيب تجري مجرى الوصف، فإذا فات الوصف المرغوب فيه يتخير كما في سائر الأوصاف، و به قال مالك وأحمد والشافعي في قول، وعن مالك وهو قول من الشافعي: لا يصح البيع، وللإمام: أنه لو لم يحط في التولية لا يبقى تولية؛ لأنها تكون بالثمن الأول، وهذا يزيد عليه فتغير التصرف فتعين الحط، في المرابحة لو لم يحط تبقى مرابحة، وإن كان الربح يتفاوت فلا يتغير التصرف فأمكن القول بالتخيير، ولو هلك المبيع قبل أن يرده، أو حدث به ما يمنع الرد لزمه بجميع الثمن المسمّى، وسقط خياره عند أبي حنيفة، وهو ا المشهور من قول محمد، ويجمع أقوالهم لفظ "فطر"، فـ"الفاء" يدل على التفصيل رمز لقول الإمام الأعظم، و"الطاء" يدل على الحط رمز لقول أبي يوسف، و"الراء" يدل على التخيير رمز لقول محمد [رمز الحقائق: ٥١/٢] (فتح) في المرابحة: أي في بيع المرابحة بأن ظهرت خيانته بإقرار أو بالبينة أو بنكوله. (ط) أو رده أي فالمشتري إن شاء أخذه بالثمن الذي قاله الخائن أو رده على البائع. (ط) وحط: أي المشتري في بيع التولية قدر الخيانة من الثمن. (طعيني)
If the seller is treacherous, etc., up to his saying: 'Deduct in tawliyah.' Its example: he bought something for nine, and said to another: 'I bought it for ten and sell it to you with a profit of a dirham.' So he bought it on that basis, then it became clear that he cheated him by one dirham. According to Abu Hanifa, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes the item for the full price, and if he wishes he leaves it. If he cheated in a tawliyah sale in this manner, he deducts the amount of treachery. According to Abu Yusuf, he deducts the amount of treachery in tawliyah, and deducts the corresponding amount from the profit in murabahah. Ahmad and al-Shafi'i said the same in one opinion, because tawliyah and murabahah are based on the first price, so it is measured accordingly. However, in tawliyah he deducts the amount of treachery, and in murabahah also from the profit proportionally, because the profit is divided on the capital and the amount of treachery. According to Muhammad, he has the option in both, because they both entered into a contract by their choice for a known price, so it is concluded with the named price, as they agreed to it. Mentioning murabahah and tawliyah is for promotion and encouragement, which are like descriptions. When the desired description is absent, he has the option as in other descriptions. Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i said the same in one opinion. From Malik, and it is also a saying of al-Shafi'i, the sale is invalid. For the Imam: if he does not deduct in tawliyah, it is no longer tawliyah because it is based on the first price, and this increases it, so the transaction changes, so deduction is necessary. In murabahah, if he does not deduct, it remains murabahah, although the profit varies, so the transaction does not change, so it is possible to say option. If the item perishes before he returns it, or something occurs that prevents return, he is liable for the full named price, and his option drops according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the well-known opinion of Muhammad. They summarized their opinions with the word 'fatar'. 'Fa'' indicates detail, symbolizing the Imam's sayling; 'ta'' indicates deduction, symbolizing Abu Yusuf's saying; 'ra'' indicates option, symbolizing Muhammad's saying. (Fath) In murabahah: i.e., in a murabahah sale when his treachery appears by confession, evidence, or refusal to take an oath. (Ta') Or return it: i.e., the buyer, if he wishes, takes it for the price stated by the treacherous seller, or returns it to the seller. (Ta') And deduct: i.e., the buyer in a tawliyah sale deducts the amount of treachery from the price. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the buyer's remedy when a seller in a fiduciary sale (tawliyah or murabahah) misrepresents the cost price. The example: seller bought for 9, claims bought for 10, and sells at 1 dirham profit; actual cost is 9. Three positions: Abu Hanifa gives the buyer a choice in murabahah—either take the item at the falsely stated price (10) or cancel. In tawliyah, he deducts the cheated amount (1) from the price. Abu Yusuf deducts the cheated amount in both tawliyah and murabahah, proportionally reducing profit in the latter. Muhammad gives the buyer an option in both, treating the profit disclosure as a mere description. The text also notes that if the item perishes before return, the option drops and the full price is due. A mnemonic 'fatar' is used: fa' for Abu Hanifa's detail (option in murabahah only), ta' for Abu Yusuf's deduction, ra' for Muhammad's option in both. The commentary by al-Tahawi and others clarifies the application.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust by misstating the cost price in a fiduciary sale.
تخيير (takhayyur): The buyer's option to either enforce the sale at stated price or rescind.
حط (hatt): Deduction of the cheated amount from the price.
تفصيل (tafsil): Detail; refers to Abu Hanifa's position of differentiating between murabahah (option) and tawliyah (deduction).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fa'
B) Ta'
C) Ra'
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers three distinct remedies for a seller's misrepresentation of cost in fiduciary sales; the mufta bihi is to give the buyer an option in murabahah and a deduction in tawliyah.

Deducting Previous Profit in Murabahah upon Repurchase
Ruling on murabahah when buyer repurchases the same item previously sold at profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on deducting previous profit in murabahah when the seller repurchases the same item, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Previous profit must be deducted if repurchasing same item? Abu Hanifa says yes; Sahibayn says no.

Murabahah is a sale built on trust (amanah). When a seller repurchases a commodity he had previously sold at profit, the question arises: should the original profit be deducted from the disclosed cost? Abu Hanifa held that suspicion of treachery requires deduction, while the two companions viewed each transaction independently. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Understand the fact pattern: a seller buys an item, sells it at profit via murabahah, then repurchases the same item at a lower price.
The key is that the same item is bought back from the same buyer.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's Ruling and Dalil
Abu Hanifa rules that all previous profit must be deducted from the cost. His reasoning: suspicion of treachery (shubhat al-khiyanah) exists because the first profit might be rescinded due to a defect.
If the first profit equals or exceeds the repurchase price, he forbids murabahah entirely (ihatah).
3
Identify Sahibayn's Ruling and Dalil
Sahibayn treat the second purchase as an entirely new transaction. The cost is simply the repurchase price; no deduction is required. They argue that each sale is independent.
This view aligns with Shafi'i and Malik.
4
Note the Special Case of Ihatat
When the first profit equals the repurchase price, Abu Hanifa prohibits murabahah completely. Sahibayn allow it, as in the normal case.
5
Recognize When All Agree
If a third party intervenes between the transactions, there is no trust issue, and all agree the last price is used. Disclosure also resolves the dispute.
Disclosure: 'I sold it for profit X, then bought it for Y' makes murabahah permissible on Y unanimously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اشتراه أي ذلك الثوب ثانيا بالثمن الأول. (ط، عيني) فإن باعه: أي فإن أراد أن يبيعه بربح ثانيا. (عيني) كل ربح قبله إلخ: هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يبيعه مرابحة على الثمن الأخير، صورته: اشترى ثوباً بعشرين ثم باعه مرابحة بثلاثين ثم اشتراه بعشرين؛ فإنه يبيعه مرابحة على عشرة، ويقول: قام علي بعشرة، وعندهما يبيعه مرابحة على العشرين وبه قال الشافعي ومالك؛ لأن هذا شراء جديد لا تعلق له بالأول، فلا يعتبر فيه ما ربحه قبل ذلك الشراء، وله أن بيع المرابحة بناء على الأمانة، وههنا شبهة الخيانة ثابتة؛ لأن الربح الذي ربحه في البيع الأول - وهو البيع بثلاثين - في معرض السقوط بفسخ البيع الأول بظهور العيب. رمز الحقائق: ٥١/٢] (فتح) وإن أحاط إلخ : أي لو اشترى ثوباً بعشرة وباعه بعشرين ثم اشتراه بعشرة، لا يبيعه مرابحة أصلاً عنده، وعندهما يبيعه مرابحة بعشرة كما في الصورة الأولى، فالخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في طرح كل ربح حصل قبله، فعنده يطرح خلافاً لهما، وكذا الخلاف في عدم جواز المرابحة أصلاً إذا أحاط الربح برأس المال عنده، خلافاً لهما، ومحله ما إذا لم يتخلل بين المشتري والبائع ثالث، فإن تخلل بينهما ثالث جاز البيع مرابحة على الثمن الأخير مطلقاً، أحاطه الربح أم لا، ولا يلزمه الطرح بالاتفاق، والخلاف أيضاً في عدم البيان، فإذا بين وقال: كنت بعته فربحت فيه كذا ثم اشتريته بكذا، أو أنا أبيعه الآن بربح اتفاقاً، وكذا لو باع بغير الجنس من الثمن الأول، فإنه يرابح على الثمن الأخير اتفاقاً. (فتح القدير، فتح) بثمنه: بأن اشترى بعشرين وباعه بأربعين مرابحة ثم اشتراه بعشرين. (عيني) مأذون: أي عبد مأذون له بالتجارة. (عيني)
Then he bought it, i.e., that garment a second time at the first price. (Ta', Ayni) Then he sold it: i.e., if he wants to sell it at profit a second time. (Ayni) Every profit before it, etc.: this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he sells it as murabahah on the last price. Its example: he bought a garment for twenty, then sold it as murabahah for thirty, then bought it for twenty; then he sells it as murabahah on ten, saying: it cost me ten. According to the two, he sells it as murabahah on twenty. Al-Shafi'i and Malik said the same, because this is a new purchase unrelated to the first, so what he profited before that purchase is not considered. And because the murabahah sale is based on trust, and here the suspicion of treachery is present, because the profit from the first sale - which was the sale for thirty - is liable to fall due to rescission of the first sale upon discovery of a defect. (Fath) And if the profit encompasses, etc.: i.e., if he bought a garment for ten, sold it for twenty, then bought it for ten, he cannot sell it as murabahah at all according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to the two, he sells it as murabahah for ten as in the first case. The dispute between the Imam and his two companions is regarding deducting every profit obtained before it. According to him, he deducts, contrary to them. Similarly, the dispute regarding the impermissibility of murabahah at all if the profit encompasses the capital according to him, contrary to them. This applies when no third party intervenes between the buyer and seller. If a third party intervenes between them, it is permissible to sell as murabahah on the last price absolutely, whether the profit encompasses or not, and he is not required to deduct by agreement. The dispute also applies when he does not disclose. If he discloses and says: I sold it and profited such-and-such, then I bought it for such-and-such, or I am now selling it at profit, by agreement. Similarly, if he sold with a different currency than the first price, then he sells as murabahah on the last price by agreement. (Fath al-Qadir, Fath) 'For its price': such that he bought for twenty and sold as murabahah for forty, then bought it for twenty. (Ayni) 'Madhun': i.e., a slave permitted to trade. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuanced scenario: a buyer purchases an item, sells it at profit via murabahah (on trust), then later repurchases the same item at a lower price. The question is: when he resells it via murabahah, should the cost be the original purchase price (which includes the first profit) or the repurchase price? Abu Hanifa holds that all profit from the first sale must be deducted because the trust is undermined—the buyer knows the seller once made a large profit, raising suspicion of overstatement. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat the second purchase as an entirely new transaction, so the cost is simply the repurchase price. This agrees with Shafi'i and Malik. The text further explains that if the first profit equals or exceeds the repurchase price (i.e., he sold for 20, bought back for 10, profit of 10 equals repurchase cost), Abu Hanifa forbids murabahah altogether because the seller would be claiming a profit that entirely consumes the capital. The companions allow it. However, if a third party intervenes—e.g., someone else bought and then sold back—there is no trust issue, and all agree on the last price. Disclosure also resolves the suspicion, allowing any price.

Key Terms
ربح (ribh): Profit gained in a murabahah sale; previous profit must be deducted per Abu Hanifa when repurchasing same item.
طرح (tarh): Deduction of previous profit from the cost price in murabahah upon repurchase.
إحاطة (ihatah): When the previous profit equals or surpasses the repurchase price, preventing murabahah completely per Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a seller bought an item for 10, sold it for 20, then bought it back for 10, can he resell it as murabahah?
A) Yes, on the cost of 10
B) Yes, on the cost of 20
C) No, because profit equals capital
D) Yes, but only if he discloses

Key Takeaway
In murabahah of a repurchased item, the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school allows the seller to use the last purchase price as cost without deducting previous profit.

Murabahah After Repurchase: Deducting Prior Profit
Expanding matn rulings on murabahah after repurchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's rule of deducting all previous profit when repurchasing an item for murabahah, and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
In murabahah after repurchase, the seller must deduct all prior profit; if profit equals capital, the sale is invalid.

Murabahah is a trust-based sale requiring full disclosure. When the seller repurchases an item and wants to resell it on murabahah, a key question arises: should the profit from the previous sale be deducted from the cost? The Hanafi school has an internal difference, with Imam Abu Hanifa insisting on full deduction to avoid any suspicion of treachery. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proofs, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
From the matn: the seller must deduct every profit made before the repurchase. If the previous profit equals the repurchase price, murabahah is not permitted.
This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they consider the repurchase a new transaction, so they do not deduct the previous profit. They allow murabahah on the last purchase price even if profit equals capital.
Al-Shafi'i and Malik agree with the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the proofs
The Sahibayn argue: 'This is a new purchase with no connection to the first.' Abu Hanifa responds: the profit from the first sale may be voided if the first buyer returns the item due to a defect, so the trust (amanah) required for murabahah is compromised.
Dalil: Both sides use qiyas, but Abu Hanifa prioritizes istihsan to preserve trust.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller's cost base is lower, reducing the profit margin. Under the Sahibayn, the cost base is higher, allowing more profit. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view.
This difference affects real business: sellers must be careful to deduct prior profits when repurchasing.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ٥١/٢] (فتح) وإن أحاط إلخ : أي لو اشترى ثوباً بعشرة وباعه بعشرين ثم اشتراه بعشرة، لا يبيعه مرابحة أصلاً عنده، وعندهما يبيعه مرابحة بعشرة كما في الصورة الأولى، فالخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في طرح كل ربح حصل قبله، فعنده يطرح خلافاً لهما، وكذا الخلاف في عدم جواز المرابحة أصلاً إذا أحاط الربح برأس المال عنده، خلافاً لهما، ومحله ما إذا لم يتخلل بين المشتري والبائع ثالث، فإن تخلل بينهما ثالث جاز البيع مرابحة على الثمن الأخير مطلقاً، أحاطه الربح أم لا، ولا يلزمه الطرح بالاتفاق، والخلاف أيضاً في عدم البيان، فإذا بين وقال: كنت بعته فربحت فيه كذا ثم اشتريته بكذا، أو أنا أبيعه الآن بربح اتفاقاً، وكذا لو باع بغير الجنس من الثمن الأول، فإنه يرابح على الثمن الأخير اتفاقاً. (T) 'and reduction': meaning the buyer in a tawliyah sale reduces the amount of treachery from the price. (T, Ayni) 'then he buys it': meaning that garment a second time at the first price. (T, Ayni) 'then he sells it': meaning he desires to sell it with profit a second time. (Ayni) 'every profit before it etc.': This according to Abu Hanifa. According to them, he sells it in murabahah at ten as in the first scenario. Thus the dispute between the Imam and his two companions is regarding deducting every profit obtained before it. So according to him, he deducts, contrary to them. Similarly, the dispute regarding the impermissibility of murabahah at all when profit encompasses the capital according to him, contrary to them. And its locus is when no third party intervenes between the buyer and seller. The dispute also regarding not disclosing. So if he discloses and says, 'I had sold it and profited such, then I bought it for such,' or 'I sell it now with profit,' unanimously. (Fath al-Qadir, Fath) 'at its price': meaning he buys for twenty and sells for forty in murabahah, then buys it for twenty. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq dealing with murabahah and tawliyah sales. The matn states the basic rules: In murabahah (a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit), the buyer has the option to take the item at the stated price or return it. In tawliyah (a sale at exactly the cost price), the buyer reduces the price by the amount of any defect. Then a scenario is given: If a person buys a garment, sells it at a profit, then repurchases it, and then wishes to resell it in murabahah, he must deduct from the new cost the profit he made in the previous sale. If the previous profit equals or exceeds the repurchase price, he cannot sell it in murabahah at all. The commentary clarifies that the buyer who repurchases gets the option. It then discusses the ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa deducts the entire previous profit, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider the new purchase a fresh transaction, so they do not deduct; the murabahah is based on the last purchase price. This is also the view of al-Shafi'i and Malik. The Hanafi response argues that there is a suspicion of breach of trust because the profit from the first sale may be invalidated if the first sale is rescinded due to a defect. Then the commentary addresses the case where profit equals capital: Abu Hanifa says murabahah is totally impermissible, while the Sahibayn say it is permitted after deducting the profit. Finally, an exception is noted: if a third party interposes between the two sales, then the repurchase is considered a new transaction by unanimous agreement, and murabahah on the last price is allowed without deduction.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the purchase price and adds an agreed profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale at exactly the cost price without profit, usually with a discount for defects.
طرح (tarh): Deduction; here, deducting the previous profit from the repurchase cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) $10 (the repurchase price, no deduction)
B) $10 minus the previous profit of $10, i.e., $0 — murabahah invalid
C) $20 (the last sale price)
D) $10 plus the previous profit of $10, i.e., $20

Key Takeaway
In murabahah after repurchase, the seller must deduct all previous profit to preserve trust; if profit equals capital, the sale is invalid.

Murabahah on a Slave's Purchase from His Master
Ruling with proof: murabahah pricing when slave sells to master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for murabahah when a permitted slave in debt sells to his master, and explain the legal reasoning based on the master's right in the slave's property.

Core Idea
A slave's sale to his master is valid but for murabahah, only the original cost counts——the excess is still in the master's ownership.

This passage addresses a unique case in murabahah: a permitted slave (ma'dhun) who is in debt buys a garment for ten dirhams and sells it to his master for fifteen. When the master wishes to resell in murabahah, he must base it on the original ten, not the fifteen. Why? Because the slave's ownership is not fully independent——the master has a right over the slave's property. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master selling the garment in murabahah after buying it from his slave. The ruling: the murabahah base price is the original price paid by the slave (10), not the price paid to the slave (15).
The sale between master and slave is valid (sahih) but with a semblance of non-existence for pricing.
2
Note the condition for validity
The slave must be in debt (madyun) with debt encompassing his value (al-dayn al-muhit). This ensures the slave has real ownership and the second sale is valid. Without debt, the slave has no ownership and the sale is void.
The text explicitly restricts the ruling to slaves with encompassing debt.
3
Trace the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The key principle: the slave is the master's property, and what is in the slave's hand is not free from the master's right. Therefore, the extra profit (5) did not fully leave the master's ownership because it remains jointly between master and slave. So only the original price (10) is considered to have left the master's ownership.
The phrase 'لأن العبد ملكه، وما في يده لا يخلو عن حقه' encapsulates the reason.
4
Apply to the reverse case
The same logic applies if the master buys from the slave (the reverse): the murabahah is still based on the original price the slave paid, because the master's payment to the slave is not a true exit from his ownership.
The text says 'وكذا العكس' (and similarly the reverse).
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Shafi'is may consider the sale to the master as a normal sale, allowing the master to use the price he paid as the base for murabahah. Hanafis differ due to the master's right over the slave's property.
This ikhtilaf arises from different views on the slave's capacity to own independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى مأذون مديون ثوباً بعشرة، وباع من سيده بخمسة عشر، يبيعه مرابحة على عشرة، وكذا العكس. يبيعه مرابحة على عشرة: لأن العقد بينهما وإن كان صحيحا ولكن له شبهة العدم؛ لأن العبد ملكه، وما في يده لا يخلو عن حقه، فبقي الاعتبار للشراء الأول، فصار يبيعه للمولى فيعتبر الثمن الأول لا غير؛ لأن الزائد عليه دائر بين المولى والعبد، فلم يتم خروجه عن ملكه فيكون الزائد على الثمن الأول باقيا على ملكه فلا يعتبر خارجا، وقيدنا بالدين المحيط ليصح العقد. أما المأذون الذي لا دين عليه فلا ملك له فلا شبهة في أن البيع الثاني لا اعتبار له، أما إذا كان له فحينئذ يكون البيع الثاني بيعا لكن مع ذلك لا اعتبار له في حق المرابحة. (فتح، شرح وقاية).
And if a permitted (ma'dhun) slave who is in debt buys a garment for ten, and sells to his master for fifteen, he sells it in murabahah at ten. And similarly the reverse. He sells it in murabahah at ten: because the contract between them, though valid, has a semblance of non-existence; because the slave is his property, and what is in his hand is not free of his master's right, so the consideration remains for the first purchase. Thus it becomes as if he sold it to the master, so only the first price is considered, because the excess is shared between the master and the slave, so it did not fully leave his ownership, so the excess over the first price remains in his ownership and is not considered as having left. And we restricted it to the encompassing debt to validate the contract. As for the permitted slave who has no debt, he has no ownership, so there is no ambiguity that the second sale is not considered. But if he has debt, then the second sale is a sale, yet despite that it is not considered for the purpose of murabahah. (Fath, Sharh al-Wiqayah).
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case in murabahah: a permitted slave (ma'dhun) who is in debt buys a garment for ten dirhams and then sells that same garment to his master for fifteen dirhams. When the master then wishes to resell the garment in murabahah, he must base the murabahah on the original purchase price of ten dirhams, not the fifteen he paid the slave. The reasoning is that the contract between the master and his slave, though valid on the surface, has a semblance of nullity because the slave is the master's property. The slave's hand is not free from the master's right due to the debt that overwhelms the slave's value. Therefore, the effective purchase is considered from the original seller, and the extra five dirhams (the profit) is not considered to have left the master's ownership because it remains jointly between master and slave. Thus, for murabahah, only the original ten dirhams count. The condition of 'debt encompassing the slave's value' is necessary to make the second sale valid in the first place. If the slave had no debt, he would have no ownership capacity, and the sale would be void. The reverse case is also covered: if the master buys from the slave, similar logic applies.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade.
مديون (madyun): In debt; a slave whose debts exceed his value ('encompassing debt').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) $150
B) $100
C) $125
D) $0, invalid sale

Key Takeaway
For murabahah, the base price is the original cost incurred by the seller, not the price paid to an intermediary whose ownership is not fully independent.

Murabahah with Master and Madhun Slave
Ruling on murabahah cost basis with permitted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct cost basis in a murabahah transaction when a permitted slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun) transacts with his master, and explain the legal reasoning (shubhat al-'adam) behind it.

Core Idea
When a permitted slave trades with his master, the second transaction is disregarded for murabahah; the original purchase price alone counts.

In the Hanafi school, the murabahah (cost-plus sale) requires the seller to disclose the true cost. But what if the item was previously sold between a master and his permitted slave? This passage clarifies that the second sale is considered legally non-existent for cost purposes, because the slave's property effectively belongs to the master. Understanding this ruling prevents errors in profit calculation and preserves the trust-based nature of murabahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the two transactions
We have a master and his permitted slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun) who has overwhelming debt. There are two sales: first, the slave buys an item for 10; second, the slave sells it to his master for 15. Or vice versa: the master buys for 10 and sells to the slave for 15. The question is: what cost should be disclosed in a subsequent murabahah?
2
Apply the principle of shubhat al-'adam
The Hanafi concept of shubhat al-'adam (semblance of non-existence) holds that a contract which is valid in form but contradictory to ownership is considered as if it did not occur for certain legal effects. Here, the slave's contract with the master is seen as the master contracting with himself, because the slave's property is ultimately the master's. Thus, the second sale is disregarded.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the apparent validity of the second contract.
3
Apply to the slave-to-master case
If the slave bought for 10 and sold to master for 15, the master's cost basis for murabahah is 10 (the slave's purchase price). The extra 5 is not considered a cost because it never truly left the master's dominion.
4
Apply to the master-to-slave case
If the master bought for 10 and sold to the slave for 15, the slave's cost basis for his murabahah is 10. The sale from master to slave is similarly disregarded.
5
Note the condition of debt and the exception
This ruling applies only when the slave is 'indebted' (madhyun) with overwhelming debt. Without debt, the slave has no ownership; the second sale is simply void, and the cost basis is irrelevant. The debt creates a semblance of independent ownership that justifies the second sale's form, but not its effect on murabahah.
The mukatab (slave on contract of manumission) is treated like the indebted permitted slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التولية والمرابحة يبيعه مرابحة على عشرة، وكذا العكس، أي البائع الثوب يبيعه مرابحة إلخ : أي إذا اشترى عبد مأذون ثوبا بعشرة وباعه من سيده بخمسة عشر يبيع المولى في بيع المرابحة على عشرة؛ لأن عقد العبد من سيده عقد السيد على ماله وملكه فيبطل هذا العقد ويبقى شراء العبد بعشرة، وذلك الشراء كشراء المولى، وقوله: "مديون" وقع اتفاقاً؛ لأنه إذا كان لا يجوز مع الدين أن يبيعه مرابحة، فمع عدم الدين أولى؛ لوجود ملك المولى فيه بالإجماع، والمكاتب كالعبد المديون المأذون له، ولو بين أنه اشتراه من عبده المأذون المديون أو من مكاتبه، له أن يبيعه مرابحة على خمسة عشر [ رمز الحقائق: ٥٢/٢] (فتح) على عشرة : لأن العقد بينهما وإن كان صحيحا ولكن له شبهة العدم؛ لأن العبد ملكه، وما في يده لا يخلو عن دین كأنه اشتراه للمولى بعشرة فيعتبر هذا لا غير. (عيني) محيط وكذا العكس : أي إذا اشترى المولى ثوبا بعشرة، ثم باعه من مأذونه المحيط دينه برقبته بخمسة عشر، فللمأذون يبيعه مرابحة على عشرة ويقول: قام علي بعشرة؛ لأن بيع المولى من عبده المأذون وشراؤه منه اعتبر عدما في حق المرابحة لثبوته مع المنافي؛ لأن العبد ملكه، وما في يده لا يخلو عن حقه فبقي الاعتبار للشراء الأول، فصار يبيعه للمولى فيعتبر الثمن الأول لا غير؛ لأن الزائد عليه دائر بين المولى والعبد، فلم يتم خروجه عن كان له فيكون الزائد على الثمن الأول باقيا على ملكه فلا يعتبر خارجا، وقيدنا بالدين المحيط ليصح العقد. أما المأذون الذي لا دين عليه فلا ملك له فلا شبهة في أن البيع الثاني لا اعتبار له، أما إذا كان له فحينئذ يكون البيع الثاني بيعا لكن مع ذلك لا اعتبار له في حق المرابحة. (فتح، شرح وقاية)
Chapter of Assignment and Cost-Plus Sale. He sells it on a cost-plus basis at ten, and likewise the reverse. That is, if a permitted slave bought a garment for ten and sold it to his master for fifteen, the master, when selling on cost-plus, sells at ten; because the slave's contract with his master is the master's contract on his own property and ownership, so this contract is voided and the slave's purchase for ten remains, and that purchase is like the master's purchase. His statement 'indebted' is accidental; because if it is not permissible with debt to sell it on cost-plus, then without debt it is even more so, due to the existence of the master's ownership by consensus. The mukatab is like the indebted permitted slave. If he discloses that he bought it from his indebted permitted slave or his mukatab, he may sell it on cost-plus at fifteen. (Fath) At ten: because the contract between them, though valid, has a semblance of non-existence; because the slave is his property, and what is in his hand is not free of debt; as if he bought it for the master for ten, so this alone is considered. (Ayni) And likewise the reverse: if the master bought a garment for ten, then sold it to his indebted permitted slave for fifteen, the slave may sell it on cost-plus at ten and say: 'It cost me ten'; because the master's sale to his permitted slave and his purchase from him are considered non-existent with respect to cost-plus, because they exist with a contradictory factor; since the slave is his property and what is in his hand is not free of his right, so the consideration remains for the first purchase, so he sells it to the master and the first price alone is considered; because the excess is shared between the master and the slave, so its departure from the master is not complete, thus the excess over the first price remains in his ownership and is not considered as outgoing. We restricted it to overwhelming debt for the contract to be valid. As for a permitted slave with no debt, he has no ownership, so there is no doubt that the second sale is disregarded. But if he has debt, then the second sale is a sale, yet it is disregarded with respect to cost-plus.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on murabahah (cost-plus sale) involving a master and his permitted slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun). The key principle: when a slave with overwhelming debt buys an item and then sells it to his master, or vice versa, the second transaction is legally considered non-existent for murabahah purposes. The cost basis for the master's murabahah is the slave's original purchase price (ten), not the price paid to the slave (fifteen). Similarly, if the master sells to the slave, the slave's cost basis is the master's original purchase price. The reason is that the slave's ownership is incomplete—his hand is subject to the master's rights and debts—so the second transaction is seen as a mere circulation within the master's property. The commentary emphasizes that this ruling applies only when the slave is indebted such that his property is effectively encumbered. Without debt, the slave has no ownership, and the sale is void. The author clarifies that the madhun slave's purchase is considered the master's purchase by istihsan (juristic preference), overriding the apparent validity of the second contract.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون ('abd ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; his transactions are valid but his property ultimately belongs to the master.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a stated profit margin.
شبهة العدم (shubhat al-'adam): A semblance of non-existence; a contract that is legally valid but considered as if it did not occur for certain legal purposes due to conflicting factors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a permitted slave with overwhelming debt buys goods for 10 dinars and sells them to his master for 15 dinars, what is the master's correct cost basis if he later sells the goods on murabahah?
A) 10 dinars
B) 15 dinars
C) 12.5 dinars
D) The market value at the time of master's sale

Key Takeaway
In murabahah involving a master and his permitted indebted slave, the second transaction is legally ignored for cost calculation; the original purchase price prevails.

Disclosure of Defects in Murabahah: Natural vs. Caused
Ruling on disclosure of defects in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for disclosing defects (ta'yib vs. ta'yib) and virginity status in murabahah sales, and explain the rationale based on the hadith against cheating.

Core Idea
Natural defects need no disclosure of prior soundness; caused defects and virginity loss must be disclosed.

In murabahah sales, the seller must disclose any existing defect because cheating is prohibited. However, the Hanafi school distinguishes between defects that occur naturally (ta'yib) and those caused by human action (ta'yib). This lesson clarifies which defects require disclosure and why, based on the principle that the price corresponds to the substance, not to descriptions or benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect type
Determine whether the defect is natural (ta'yib, e.g., disease, accident) or caused (ta'yib, e.g., cutting). For natural defects, the seller must disclose the defect itself but need not mention that the item was sound before. For caused defects, full disclosure is required.
The term 'ta'yib' here means defect from heavenly affliction, while 'ta'yib' means defect from human action.
2
Analyze the thayyib/virgin distinction
Loss of virginity in a thayyib (non-virgin) is not considered a defect; in a virgin (bikr), it is a caused defect requiring disclosure.
The hymen is part of the substance, so its removal by action is a defect.
3
Apply the hadith against cheating
The hadith 'Man ghashshana falaysa minna' makes concealing defects haram. Thus, the seller must disclose the defect itself even if he need not mention prior soundness.
The hadith is ahad, so its obligation is zanni, but the ruling is wajib.
4
Consider the intra-madhhab disagreement
Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar hold that loss of an eye, even if natural, must be disclosed. The Hanafi majority view is that this disagreement is limited to the eye; for other natural defects, consensus exists.
The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.
5
Determine buyer's recourse
If the seller fails to disclose a wajib disclosure, the buyer has the option to return the item (khiyar al-'ayb) upon discovering the deception.
This ensures the sale is not void but rescindable at the buyer's option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرابح بلا بيان بالتعيب ووطء التيب، وبيان بالتعييب ووطء البكر، ويرابح إلخ أي يبيع بيع المرابحة من غير ذكر أنه كان اشتراه سليماً بكذا من الثمن، ثم أصابه العيب عنده بعد ذلك كما إذا اشترى جارية سليمة فاعورت، فيبيعها مرابحة بلا ذكر أنها كانت سليمة، وأما بيان نفس العيب، فواجب لابد منه؛ لقوله عليه: "من غشنا فليس منا ، فلا يجوز إخفاؤه، والمراد بالتعيب تعيب المبيع عنده من غير صنعة بآفة سماوية، ووجه المرابحة من غير ذكر التعيب أن الفائت وصف وهو لا يقابله شيء من الثمن. وقوله: "ووطء الثيب" أي وكذا يرابح بلا بيان الوطء في الجارية الثيب؛ لأنه لم يحتبس عنده شيء يقابله الثمن؛ لأن الفائت وصف، ومنافع البضع لا يدخل في الثمن بمجرد العقد بل تكون تبعاً؛ لأنه لا يكون لها حصة معلومة من الثمن، والثمن لا يزيد بسبب الوصف، ولا ينقص بفواته، ولهذا لو حدث بالمبيع عيب، وتركه قبل القبض لا يسقط شيء من الثمن بمجرد العقد، غير أن المشتري يتخير بين أخذه بكل الثمن وتركه، وعن أبي يوسف في التعيب: أنه لا يبيع من غير بيان كما إذا حصل من فعله وبه قال الشافعي وزفر، وهذا الخلاف يختص بالاعورار، أما الوطء فلا يلزم بيانه اتفاقاً إذا لم ينقصها الوطء. (عيني، فتح) وببيان إلخ: أي يرابح بذكر التعييب أي العيب الذي حدث بفعل فاعل مختار سواء كان الفاعل هو المشتري أو غيره؛ لأنه صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف، فيقابله شيء من الثمن، ووطء البكر تعييب؛ لأن العذرة جزء من العين، فإزالتها تعييب لها، ولو تعيب المبيع بفعله في نفسه كما إذا فقأ عين نفسه، فهو بمنزلة ما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية، فجاز أن يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان ثم في كل موضع ليس له أن يبيعه مرابحة إلا ببيان، فإن لم يبين فللمشتري أن یرده عليه إذا علم خيانته، وعلى هذا لو اشترى ثوباً فأصابه قرض فأرة أو حرق نار يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان [رمز الحقائق: ٥٣/٢] (فتح) بالتعييب والفرق بين التعييب والتعيب أن التعيب ما كان بآفة سماوية بلا فعل فاعل والتعييب ما كان بفعل فاعل مختار أعم من أن يكون هو المشتري أو غيره إلا ما كان بفعل المبيع. (فتح)
And he sells on cost-plus without disclosure of natural defect and of intercourse with a thayyib, and with disclosure of caused defect and of intercourse with a virgin. That is, he sells on cost-plus without mentioning that he had bought it sound at such a price and then a defect occurred to him after that, like if he bought a sound slave-girl and she became one-eyed, so he sells her on cost-plus without mentioning she was sound. As for disclosing the defect itself, it is obligatory and inevitable due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever cheats us is not of us,' so concealing it is not permissible. What is meant by 'ta'yib' is a defect occurring to the sold item at his place without any action, by a heavenly affliction. The basis of selling on cost-plus without mentioning the defect is that what is lost is a description, and nothing of the price corresponds to it. His statement 'and intercourse with a thayyib' means likewise he sells on cost-plus without disclosing intercourse in the case of a thayyib slave-girl; because nothing corresponding to the price is held back at his place; because what is lost is a description, and the benefits of the vulva are not included in the price merely by the contract, but are subsidiary; they have no known share of the price, and the price does not increase due to the description nor decrease by its loss. For this reason, if a defect occurs in the sold item and he leaves it before delivery, nothing of the price is dropped merely by the contract, but the buyer has the option to take it for the full price or leave it. From Abu Yusuf concerning natural defect: he does not sell without disclosure when it occurs by his action, and al-Shafi'i and Zufar said the same. This disagreement is specific to loss of an eye; as for intercourse, disclosure is not necessary by consensus if intercourse did not reduce her value. With disclosure of caused defect: he sells on cost-plus mentioning the defect caused by a free agent's action, whether the agent is the buyer or another; because it becomes an object of destruction, so something of the price corresponds to it. Intercourse with a virgin is a caused defect because the hymen is part of the substance, its removal is a defect. If the sold item is defective by his own action, like if he gouged out his own eye, it is like a defect by a heavenly affliction, so he may sell it on cost-plus without disclosure. Then in every case where he may not sell on cost-plus except with disclosure, if he does not disclose, the buyer has the option to return it upon learning of his deception. And likewise if he bought a garment and a mouse gnawed it or fire burned it, he sells it on cost-plus without disclosure. The difference between ta'yib and ta'yib: ta'yib is what is by a heavenly affliction without action of an agent; ta'yib is by action of a free agent, whether the agent is the buyer or another, except what is by the action of the sold item itself.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage details the disclosure requirements in murabahah sales regarding defects. It distinguishes between 'ta'yib' (natural defect caused by heavenly affliction) and 'ta'yib' (defect caused by human action). For natural defects and intercourse with a thayyib (non-virgin), the seller need not disclose them because they are mere description loss not represented by a portion of the price. However, the text emphasizes that disclosing the existence of a defect is obligatory (wajib) due to the hadith against cheating. For defects caused by human action (e.g., gouging an eye) and defloration of a virgin, the seller must disclose because these affect the substance and correspond to a portion of the price. A key nuance: if the defect is self-inflicted by the buyer, it is treated like a natural defect. The passage also notes a disagreement: Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar hold that loss of an eye caused by the seller must be disclosed, while the majority Hanafi view distinguishes. Ultimately, the buyer has the right to return the item if deception is discovered.

Key Terms
التعيب (al-ta'yib): A natural defect occurring without human action, by heavenly affliction (e.g., disease, accident).
التعييب (al-ta'yib): A defect caused by the action of a free agent (human action), e.g., cutting, piercing.
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin (woman who has had intercourse).
بكر (bikr): A virgin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man buys a thayyib slave-girl who later becomes one-eyed due to illness. He wants to sell her on murabahah. According to the Hanafi school, must he disclose that she was originally sound or that she is now one-eyed?
A) He must disclose both the original soundness and the current defect.
B) He must disclose the current defect (one-eyed) but not that she was sound.
C) He need not disclose anything; he can sell as if she were sound.
D) He must disclose that she was sound but not the defect.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, the seller must disclose the defect itself, but for natural defects, need not mention prior soundness; for caused defects and virginity loss, disclosure is mandatory.

Murabahah Without Disclosing Credit Purchase
Ruling on buyer's option due to undisclosed credit in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a seller fails to disclose a credit purchase in murabahah and explain the buyer's option using the analogy to two combined items.

Core Idea
Failure to disclose credit in murabahah gives the buyer an option to rescind.

Murabahah is a trust-based sale where the seller must disclose the original cost. If the seller bought on credit (nasi'ah) but sells at cost-plus without mentioning the credit, the buyer is deceived. The Hanafi school rules that the sale is valid but the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either keep the item at the stated price or return it. This option is derived by analogy to selling two items together without price separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the rule
Read the passage and note the scenario: seller buys an item on credit for 1000, sells it on murabahah with profit 100, but does not disclose the credit. The buyer later discovers this. The ruling: the sale is valid but the buyer has the option (khiyar) to accept or return.
The option is due to the undisclosed credit term, not because the sale is void.
2
Understand the proof (qiyas)
The passage explains the reasoning: credit (the term) is like an additional item because it increases the price. If two items are sold together without price separation, the buyer has an option due to ambiguity. Here, the credit term is analogized to a second item. So the buyer has the same option.
The analogy is based on 'shabah' (resemblance) that the credit term is like an extra commodity.
3
Apply to tawliyah
The same option applies to tawliyah (assignment sale at cost) because both murabahah and tawliyah depend on the first price. If the first price was on credit and not disclosed, the buyer retains the option.
Tawliyah is a sale without profit, so disclosure of the original price is equally critical.
4
Note the consequence if the item is destroyed
If the buyer destroys the item or it perishes before discovering the deception, he still learns and may seek recourse, e.g., claiming damages or returning the equivalent value.
The option is not lost by destruction; the buyer can still demand justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى بألف نسيئة، وباع بربح مائة ولم يبيّن خير المشتري، فإن أتلف فعلم . ولو اشترى إلخ أي إذا اشترى غلاماً بألف درهم نسيئة، فباعه بربح مائة، ولم يبين للمشتري أنه كان اشتراه إلى أجل، فعلم المشتري ذلك، فله الخيار إن شاء رده، وإن شاء قبل؛ لأن للأجل شبهاً بالمبيع، فإنه يزاد في الثمن لأجل الأجل، والشبهة في هذا الباب ملحقة بالحقيقة، فصار كأنه اشترى شيئين، وباع أحدهما مرابحة بثمنهما، فيجب الاحتراز عن مثل هذه الخيانة في المرابحة وكذا يثبت له الخيار في التولية إذا علم أن الثمن الأول كان مؤجلاً؛ لأن التولية والمرابحة تبتنيان على الثمن الأول. (عيني، وعناية) ولم يبين: أنه اشتراه نسيئة فعلم المشتري ذلك. (ط) مسكين خير المشتري بين أن يأخذه بكل الثمن أو يتركه. (ط، عيني) فإن أتلف أي المشتري المبيع أو تلف بنفسه. (ط)
And if he bought for a thousand on credit, and sold at a profit of one hundred and did not disclose, the buyer has the option; if he destroys it then he knows. That is, if he bought a slave for one thousand dirhams on credit, then sold him at a profit of one hundred, and did not disclose to the buyer that he had bought him on credit, and the buyer learns that, he has the option: if he wishes he may return him, and if he wishes he may accept; because the term (credit) has a similarity to the sold item, for the price is increased due to the term, and similarity in this chapter is attached to reality, so it is as if he bought two things and sold one of them on cost-plus at the price of both. So it is necessary to guard against such cheating in murabahah. Likewise, the option is established for him in tawliyah (assignment) if he learns that the first price was deferred, because tawliyah and murabahah are based on the first price. 'Did not disclose' means he bought it on credit and the buyer learns that. The buyer has the option between taking it for the full price or leaving it. If he destroys it, i.e., the buyer destroys the sold item or it perishes by itself (then he is informed afterwards).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of murabahah where the seller originally bought the item on credit (nasi'ah) but sells it on cost-plus without informing the buyer of that credit. Because credit terms affect the price (a deferred price is often higher than cash), the failure to disclose is a form of cheating. The buyer, upon discovering the credit, has the option (khiyar) to either keep the item at the stated sale price or return it. The reasoning: the credit period is analogous to an additional component in the sale; it is as if the seller bought two items (the good and the credit facility) and sold one (the good) at a price covering both. Therefore, the same option applies in tawliyah (assignment sale). The passage also notes that if the buyer destroys the item before learning of the deception, he is still informed and may seek recourse. The ruling underscores the principle of full transparency in trust-based sales.

Key Terms
نسيئة (nasi'ah): A sale on credit/deferred payment.
خيار (khiyar): Option or right of choice given to a party in a contract, here the buyer's option to annul due to undisclosed credit.
تولية (tawliyah): Assignment sale where the seller sells at cost without profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer has no rights; the sale is valid.
B) The buyer must still pay 1100 dirhams.
C) The buyer has the option to return the goods and cancel the sale.
D) The buyer can deduct the credit cost from the price.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, full disclosure of the original purchase terms — including whether it was on credit — is required; failure gives the buyer the option to rescind.

Disclosing Heavenly Defects in Murabahah
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disclosing a heavenly defect in a Murabahah sale and explain the rationale that the lost description does not correspond to part of the price.

Core Idea
A heavenly defect need not be disclosed in Murabahah because what is lost is a description, not a price component.

Murabahah is a trust-based sale requiring honesty about the cost. However, Hanafi jurists distinguish between defects that affect the physical essence and those that affect only a description. This lesson examines when a seller may omit mentioning a heavenly defect in the Murabahah statement, the proof from hadith, and the rationale based on the nature of price and description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Murabahah disclosure rule
Read the passage to determine when the seller must disclose a defect in the Murabahah statement. Note that the passage distinguishes between defects from heavenly afflictions and those from human acts.
The key phrase is 'wa yurabihu bi la bayan al-ta'abbud'.
2
Locate the hadith proof
Find the hadith cited: 'Man ghashana falaysa minna.' Understand that the hadith prohibits cheating, which includes concealing defects. However, the Hanafi interpretation limits this to defects that affect the price component.
The hadith is quoted in the commentary as the proof for wajib disclosure of the defect itself.
3
Analyze the description vs. price distinction
Understand the rationale: a heavenly defect (e.g., becoming one-eyed) is a loss of a description (wasf), and nothing of the price corresponds to a description. Therefore, mentioning it in the Murabahah statement is not required, though the defect itself must be disclosed if asked.
The phrase 'al-fa'it wasf wa huwa la yuqabiluhu shay' min al-thaman' is the central reasoning.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and practical consequence
Identify the disagreement: Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar hold that any defect caused by the seller's act must be disclosed. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) allow non-disclosure even for such defects in the Murabahah statement, though the defect itself must still be disclosed.
The practical consequence: the buyer still has the option of return (khiyar al-'ayb) upon discovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرابح بلا بيان بالتعيب ووطء التيب، وبيان بالتعييب ووطء البكر. أي يبيع بيع المرابحة من غير ذكر أنه كان اشتراه سليماً بكذا من الثمن، ثم أصابه العيب عنده بعد ذلك كما إذا اشترى جارية سليمة فاعورت، فيبيعها مرابحة بلا ذكر أنها كانت سليمة، وأما بيان نفس العيب، فواجب لابد منه؛ لقوله عليه: "من غشنا فليس منا"، فلا يجوز إخفاؤه، والمراد بالتعيب تعيب المبيع عنده من غير صنعة بآفة سماوية، ووجه المرابحة من غير ذكر التعيب أن الفائت وصف وهو لا يقابله شيء من الثمن. وقوله: "ووطء الثيب" أي وكذا يرابح بلا بيان الوطء في الجارية الثيب؛ لأنه لم يحتبس عنده شيء يقابله الثمن؛ لأن الفائت وصف، ومنافع البضع لا يدخل في الثمن بمجرد العقد بل تكون تبعاً؛ لأنه لا يكون لها حصة معلومة من الثمن، والثمن لا يزيد بسبب الوصف، ولا ينقص بفواته، ولهذا لو حدث بالمبيع عيب، وتركه قبل القبض لا يسقط شيء من الثمن بمجرد العقد، غير أن المشتري يتخير بين أخذه بكل الثمن وتركه، وعن أبي يوسف في التعيب: أنه لا يبيع من غير بيان كما إذا حصل من فعله وبه قال الشافعي وزفر، وهذا الخلاف يختص بالاعورار، أما الوطء فلا يلزم بيانه اتفاقاً إذا لم ينقصها الوطء.
And he sells on Murabahah without disclosing [the occurrence of] a defect and intercourse with a non-virgin; and he discloses a human-caused defect and intercourse with a virgin. That is, he sells as Murabahah without mentioning that he had bought it sound for such-and-such price, then a defect occurred with him after that, like if he bought a sound slave-girl and she became one-eyed, he sells her on Murabahah without mentioning she was sound. As for disclosing the defect itself, it is obligatory and unavoidable due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'He who cheats us is not of us.' So it is not permissible to conceal it. The intended meaning of 'defect' (ta'abbud) is a defect occurring with him without any act, by a heavenly affliction. The rationale for Murabahah without mentioning the defect is that what is lost is a description, and nothing of the price corresponds to it. His statement: 'and intercourse with a non-virgin' means likewise he sells on Murabahah without disclosing intercourse with a non-virgin slave-girl, because nothing stayed with him that corresponds to the price; because what is lost is a description, and the benefits of the vulva do not enter into the price by mere contract but are subordinate, because they have no known share of the price, and the price does not increase due to description nor decrease by its loss. Hence, if a defect occurs in the sold item and he leaves it before taking delivery, nothing of the price is waived by mere contract, but the buyer has the choice between taking it for the full price or leaving it. From Abu Yusuf regarding a human-caused defect: he does not sell without disclosure when it occurred by his own act, and al-Shafi'i and Zufar said the same. This disagreement is specific to becoming one-eyed. As for intercourse, its disclosure is not required by consensus if did not diminish her value.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of Tadwil and Murabahah. The author states the general rule: when selling on Murabahah (cost-plus-profit sale), the seller must disclose any defect that occurred with him, because concealing it is cheating (ghish) and forbidden by the hadith. However, if the defect occurred by a heavenly affliction (e.g., the item became one-eyed) and not by the seller's act, then he may sell on Murabahah without mentioning that specific defect, because what is lost is a description (wasf) and no part of the price corresponds to the loss of a description. The same applies to defloration of a non-virgin slave-girl, since the benefit of intercourse is subordinate and has no fixed share in the price. The buyer still has the option (khiyar al-'ayb) to return the item upon discovering the defect after purchase, but the seller is not required to disclose it upfront. Abu Yusuf differs: if the defect occurred by the seller's own act, then disclosure is necessary; this disagreement is only for defects like becoming one-eyed. For defloration, there is consensus that disclosure is not required if the value is not reduced.

Key Terms
التعيب (al-ta'abbud): A defect occurring in the sold item by a heavenly affliction (e.g., fire, flood) without any human act.
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses his cost price and adds a known profit; the buyer pays the cost plus profit.
البضع (al-bud'): The vulva or intercourse; its benefits are considered subordinate in the contract and not part of the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a seller not required to disclose a heavenly defect (ta'abbud) in a Murabahah sale?
A) Because the defect is obvious and the buyer can see it.
B) Because what is lost is a description, and no part of the price corresponds to it.
C) Because the hadith on cheating only applies to intentional fraud.
D) Because the buyer has no option to return the item.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a heavenly defect need not be mentioned in the Murabahah statement because the loss is a description, not a price component, but concealing the defect itself is prohibited.

Murabahah: Disclosure of Human-Caused Defects
Ruling on selling on Murabahah while disclosing a defect caused by human action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'yib and ta'abbud and apply the ruling on disclosure in Murabahah sales.

Core Idea
A human-caused defect in Murabahah must be disclosed; a heavenly defect does not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the critical distinction between defects caused by human action (ta'yib) and those from natural causes (ta'abbud) in Murabahah sales. Understanding this distinction is essential for valid Murabahah contracts, as failure to disclose a human-caused defect constitutes cheating and gives the buyer the right of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being sold on Murabahah and the obligation to disclose defects. The ruling is that disclosure of a human-caused defect (ta'yib) is wajib.
Ta'yib is defined as a defect occurring by the act of a free agent.
2
Distinguish ta'yib from ta'abbud
Classify the defect based on its cause: ta'yib is caused by a free agent; ta'abbud is caused by a heavenly affliction without human action.
The cause determines whether disclosure is required.
3
Apply the qiyas to examples
Analyze how the rule is applied to specific cases: defloration of a virgin is ta'yib (hymen is part of the substance); a mouse gnawing or fire burning is ta'abbud.
The sold item causing its own defect is treated like ta'abbud.
4
Note exceptions and consequences
If the defect is caused by the sold item itself, it is like a heavenly defect and no disclosure is needed. If the seller fails to disclose when required, the buyer has the right to return the item upon discovering the cheat.
The exception is based on the absence of a human agent.
5
Evaluate the underlying proof
The proof is the hadith 'Whoever cheats us is not from us' (man ghasshana falaysa minna). Concealing a human-caused defect is a form of cheating because it has a known compensatory value.
The dalil is a general prohibition of cheating applied via qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وببيان إلخ: أي يرابح بذكر التعييب أي العيب الذي حدث بفعل فاعل مختار سواء كان الفاعل هو المشتري أو غيره؛ لأنه صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف، فيقابله شيء من الثمن، ووطء البكر تعييب؛ لأن العذرة جزء من العين، فإزالتها تعييب لها، ولو تعيب المبيع بفعله في نفسه كما إذا فقأ عين نفسه، فهو بمنزلة ما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية، فجاز أن يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان ثم في كل موضع ليس له أن يبيعه مرابحة إلا ببيان، فإن لم يبين فللمشتري أن یرده عليه إذا علم خيانته، وعلى هذا لو اشترى ثوباً فأصابه قرض فأرة أو حرق نار يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان [رمز الحقائق: ٥٣/٢] (فتح) بالتعييب والفرق بين التعييب والتعيب أن التعيب ما كان بآفة سماوية بلا فعل فاعل والتعييب ما كان بفعل فاعل مختار أعم من أن يكون هو المشتري أو غيره إلا ما كان بفعل المبيع.
And with disclosure etc.: That is, he sells on Murabahah mentioning ta'yib, i.e., the defect that occurred by the act of a free agent, whether the agent is the buyer or another; because it has become targeted for destruction, so something of the price corresponds to it. And intercourse with a virgin is ta'yib because the hymen is part of the 'ayn (substance), so its removal is a defect on it. If the sold item is defective by his own act, like if he gouged out his own eye, it is like a heavenly defect, so it is permissible to sell it on Murabahah without disclosure. Then, in every case where he may not sell on Murabahah except with disclosure, if he does not disclose, the buyer can return it when he knows of his cheating. And similarly, if he bought a cloth and a mouse gnawed it or fire burned it, he sells it on Murabahah without disclosure. The difference between ta'yib and ta'abbud is that ta'abbud is what is by a heavenly affliction without the act of an agent, and ta'yib is what is by the act of a free agent, whether the agent is the buyer or another, except what is by the act of the sold item itself.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling for human-caused defects (ta'yib). When the defect results from a deliberate act of a free agent—whether the seller, buyer, or a third party—the defect is considered 'targeted for destruction' (maqsudan bi al-italaf), so it is compensated by the price. Therefore, the seller must disclose it in the Murabahah statement. Defloration of a virgin is an example: the hymen is part of the substance ('ayn), so its removal is a defect requiring disclosure. However, if the defect is caused by the sold item itself (e.g., the animal injures itself), it is treated like a heavenly defect and need not be disclosed. The buyer still has the option to return if the seller cheats by not disclosing when required. The text also gives examples: if a cloth is gnawed by a mouse or burned, these are heavenly defects (no human agent), so no disclosure needed. The distinction is based on whether a human agent caused the defect.

Key Terms
التعييب (al-ta'yib): A defect occurring in the sold item due to the act of a free agent (human), which is compensated by part of the price.
العذرة (al-'adhrah): The hymen; considered part of the substance ('ayn) of a virgin slave-girl, so its removal is a ta'yib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between ta'yib and ta'abbud in a Murabahah sale?
A) Ta'yib is always from the seller's act; ta'abbud is from the buyer's act.
B) Ta'yib is caused by a free agent; ta'abbud is by a heavenly affliction.
C) Ta'yib requires disclosure; ta'abbud never requires disclosure.
D) Ta'yib reduces the price; ta'abbud increases the price.

Key Takeaway
A defect caused by a human agent (ta'yib) must be disclosed in Murabahah; a heavenly defect (ta'abbud) does not require disclosure.

Murabahah Without Disclosing Credit Term
Ruling on selling on Murabahah without disclosing that the purchase was on credit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-disclosure of a credit term in Murabahah and apply the khiyar option to a given scenario.

Core Idea
In Murabahah, a credit term resembles a commodity because it affects price; non-disclosure gives the buyer an option to rescind.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Murabahah (cost-plus sale). When a seller buys on credit and then sells on Murabahah without revealing that, the buyer has an option if he learns the truth. The reasoning uses the Hanafi concept of shabah (resemblance), treating the term as akin to a commodity. Understanding this protects against hidden price increases due to deferred payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is: seller buys an item on credit (nasi'ah), then sells it on Murabahah without disclosing the credit term. The ruling: if buyer discovers, he has the option (khiyar) to return or accept.
The contract is not automatically void; it remains valid unless the buyer exercises the option.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas khafi (hidden analogy): the credit term is not a commodity but resembles one because it affects the price. This is supported by the principle that similarity (shabah) is annexed to reality in this chapter.
No direct text (nass) is cited; the ruling is derived via istihsan based on shabah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link: In a normal Murabahah, the seller must disclose the original cost. If the original purchase was on credit, the price was likely higher due to the term. Concealing this is cheating (khayanah). Therefore, the term is treated as part of the 'cost' for disclosure purposes.
The Hanafi maxim: 'al-shabah mulhaq bi al-haqiqah' (resemblance is annexed to reality).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This passage does not mention any disagreement. The cross-madhhab status is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) within the Hanafi school.
Some later Hanafi scholars may discuss details, but the core ruling is settled.
5
Evaluate the thamarah (practical consequence)
The practical result: the buyer can rescind the sale and return the item, or accept it at the stated price. This remedy deters the seller from hiding the credit term.
The same rule applies to Tadwil (assignment sale) because both are based on the first price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى إلخ أي إذا اشترى غلاماً بألف درهم نسيئة، فباعه بربح مائة، ولم يبين للمشتري أنه كان اشتراه إلى أجل، فعلم المشتري ذلك، فله الخيار إن شاء رده، وإن شاء قبل؛ لأن للأجل شبهاً بالمبيع، فإنه يزاد في الثمن لأجل الأجل، والشبهة في هذا الباب ملحقة بالحقيقة، فصار كأنه اشترى شيئين، وباع أحدهما مرابحة بثمنهما، فيجب الاحتراز عن مثل هذه الخيانة في المرابحة وكذا يثبت له الخيار في التولية إذا علم أن الثمن الأول كان مؤجلاً؛ لأن التولية والمرابحة تبتنيان على الثمن الأول.
And if he bought, etc.: That is, if he bought a slave for one thousand dirhams on credit, then sold it with a profit of one hundred, and did not disclose to the buyer that he had bought it on a term, and the buyer learns that, then he has the option: if he wishes he may return it, and if he wishes he may accept; because the term has a similarity to the sold commodity, as the price is increased due to the term, and similarity in this chapter is annexed to reality, so it becomes as if he bought two things and sold one of them on Murabahah at the price of both. Hence, one must guard against such cheating in Murabahah. Similarly, he has the option in Tadwil if he learns that the first price was deferred, because Tadwil and Murabahah are based on the first price.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where a seller buys an item on credit (nasi'ah) and then sells it on Murabahah (cost-plus-profit) without disclosing that his own purchase was on credit. The credit term is not a real commodity, but it has a 'shabah' (resemblance) to a commodity because it affects the price—sellers charge more for deferred payment. Since the Murabahah is a trust sale based on disclosure, concealing the credit term is a form of cheating (khayanah). Therefore, once the buyer discovers the truth, he has the option (khiyar) either to return the item and be released from the contract, or to accept it at the stated price. The same rule applies to Tadwil (assignment sale). The reasoning is that the credit term, though not actually a commodity, is treated as one in this context due to its effect on the price.

Key Terms
النسيئة (al-nasi'ah): Deferred payment sale; buying on credit with a term.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Resemblance; here, the credit term is similar to a commodity because it affects the price, so it is treated as if it were part of the purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the buyer get an option if the seller did not disclose that the original purchase was on credit?
A) Because the credit term is a hidden defect in the item itself.
B) Because the credit term resembles a commodity and affects the price, so nondisclosure is cheating.
C) Because the seller sinned by buying on credit.
D) Because the buyer assumed the price was cash and is now surprised.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, the seller must disclose if the original purchase was on credit; nondisclosure gives the buyer an option to rescind the contract.
