(فتح)] لأنها لا تدخل تحت المعيار، فلم توجد العلة خلافاً للثلاثة، وقال صاحب "الهداية": وما دون نصف الصاع فهو في حكم الحفنة؛ لأنه لم يرد التقدير في الشرع بما دون نصف الصاع، وجاز التقدير بنصف صاع في صدقة الفطر، وروى المعلى عن محمد: أنه يكره التمرة بالتمرتين وقال كل شيء حرم في الكثير فالقليل منه حرام، وهذا هو الصحيح لصيانة أموال الناس؛ إذ عدم التقدير في الشرع بما دون نصف الصاع لا يستلزم إهدار التفاوت، لاسيما إذا اتخذ الحفنة بالحفنتين وسيلة إلى بيع نحو الكر بالكرين. (عيني، فتح) (ص 322) والجوزة بالجوزتين، والتمرة بالتمرتين، والفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما، واللحم بالحيوان، والكرباس بالقطن، والرّطب بالرطب أو بالتمر متماثلاً. والفلس: أي وصح أيضاً بيع الفلس بالفلسين. بأعيانهما : [ أي صح الحفنة بالحفنتين حال كونهما معينين. (عيني،مسكين)] يتعلق بجملة هذه المسائل من بيع الحفنة إلى بيع الفلس، وقال محمد: لا يجوز بيع الفلس بالفلسين؛ لأنه ثمن فصار كالدراهم بالدرهمين فلا يتعين بالتعيين، وبه قال الشافعي، وللشيخين: أنه باصطلاح الناس، وقد خرج عن الثمنية باصطلاح المتعاقدين تصحيحاً لتصرفهما بخلاف الدراهم؛ لأنها خلقت ثمناً، وإنما قيد بقوله : "بأعيانهما ؛ لأنهما إذا كانا بغير أعيانهما أو أحدهما بغير عينه لا يجوز اتفاقاً؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا. رمز الحقائق: ٥٩/٢] (فتح) واللحم إلخ : أي وصح بيع اللحم بالحيوان عند الشيخين، سواء كان من جنسه بأن باع لحم شاة بشاة، أو من خلاف جنسه بأن باع لحم بعير بشاة، وقال محمد والشافعي: لا يصح بيع اللحم بالحيوان من جنسه إلا أن يكون اللحم المفرز أكثر من الذي في الحيوان ليكون اللحم بمقابلة ما فيه من اللحم والباقي بالسقط؛ لنهيه عليه عن بيع اللحم بالحيوان، رواه مالك في "الموطأ"، ولأنهما جنس واحد، ولهذا لا يجوز بيع أحدهما بالآخر نسيئة فكذا متفاضلا ولهما أنه باع الموزون بغير الموزون؛ لأن الحيوان ليس بموزون، فيجوز كيف ما كان، وعند أحمد: لا يجوز، ولا يجوز بالنسيئة إجماعاً، ولو اشترى شاة حية بشاة مذبوحة يجوز في قولهم جميعا. (عيني، فتح) والكرباس : بكسر الكاف ثوب من القطن الأبيض. (فتح) بالقطن أي وصح بيع الثوب من القطن بالقطن مطلقا سواء كان متساويا أو متفاضلا، وكذا بالغزل لاختلافهما جنسا، ولو باع القطن بالغزل جاز عند محمد لاختلاف الجنس؛ لأن الغزل لا ينقض فيعود قطنا، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يجوز إلا متساوياً؛ لأن غزل القطن قطن، وقول محمد أظهر ، ولو باع المحلوج بغير المحلوج جاز إذا علم أن الخالص أكثر مما في الآخر وإلا لا. رمز الحقائق: ٥٩/٢، ٦٠] (فتح) والرطب إلخ: أي كيلاً بكيل عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: لا يجوز، وبه قالت الثلاثة؛ لقوله عليها حين سئل عنه: أينقص إذا جف، فقيل: نعم، فقال عليه: "لا إذا" فأفسد البيع، وأشار إلى العلة وهي النقصان، فلا يجوز بيع الرطب بالتمر، وقوله عليها في الحديث المشهور : التمر بالتمر مثلاً بمثل"، والرطب تمر ، فيجوز بيعه بالتمر متماثلاً، والدليل على أنه تمر ما روي أنه عليها حين أهدي عليه رطب، قال: "أو كل تمر خيبر "هكذا؟" سماه تمراً؛ ولأنه إن كان تمراً جاز بيعه بأول الحديث: "التمر بالتمر مثلاً بمثل"، وإن كان غير تمر، فبآخر الحديث وهو قوله عليه: "إذا اختلف النوعان، فبيعوا كيف شئتم"، وما رووه لم يصح على ما قيل، ولو صح فخبر الواحد لا يعارض به المشهور. (عيني، فتح) أو بالتمر إلخ : أي بيع الرطب بالتمر حال كونه متماثلا أي كيلا بكيل، وعندهما: لا يصح. (عيني)
And the sale of a handful for two handfuls is valid, and an apple for two apples, and an egg for two eggs, and a walnut for two walnuts, and a date for two dates, and a fals (coin) for two fals specifically identified, and meat for an animal, and a cotton cloth for cotton, and fresh dates for fresh dates or for dry dates equally. (Fath) A handful: [with fatha on the first letter and sukun on the fa, meaning the fill of two hands as in al-Sihah, and in al-Qamus and al-Nihaya: fill of the hand. (Fath)] Because it does not fall under a standard measure, so the cause (of riba) is not present, contrary to the three (schools). And the author of al-Hidayah said: what is less than half a sa' is legally like a handful because no legal measure is set for less than half a sa', but a half sa' measure is used for sadaqat al-fitr. And al-Mu'alla narrated from Muhammad that he disapproved of a date for two dates and said: anything prohibited in large quantity, its small quantity is also prohibited, and this is correct for protecting people's wealth; for the absence of a legal measure for less than half a sa' does not necessitate disregarding disparity, especially if the handful-for-two-handfuls transaction becomes a means to sell something like a kurr for two kurrs. (Ayni, Fath) (p. 322) And a walnut for two walnuts, and a date for two dates, and a fals for two fals specifically identified, and meat for an animal, and a cotton cloth for cotton, and fresh dates for fresh dates or for dry dates equally. And 'al-fals': i.e., the sale of a fals for two fals is also valid. 'By their specific identities': [i.e., the sale of a handful for two handfuls is valid when they are specifically identified. (Ayni, Miskeen)] It applies to all these issues from the sale of a handful to the sale of a fals. Muhammad said: the sale of a fals for two fals is not valid because it is a price (thaman) and thus becomes like dirhams for two dirhams, so it is not specified by specification. al-Shafi'i also said this. For the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): it is by people's convention, and it has left the category of being a price by the contracting parties' convention to validate their transaction, unlike dirhams because they were created as price. He restricted it with 'by their specific identities' because if they are not specific identities, or one of them is not specific, it is not valid by consensus because it leads to riba. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 59/2] (Fath) 'And meat, etc.': i.e., the sale of meat for an animal is valid according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), whether it is of the same genus (e.g., selling mutton for a sheep) or of a different genus (e.g., selling camel meat for a sheep). Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: the sale of meat for an animal of the same genus is not valid unless the separated meat is more than that in the animal so that the meat is in exchange for the meat in the animal and the remainder is for the offal (saqt); due to the Prophet's prohibition of selling meat for an animal, narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta', and because they are one genus, so one cannot be sold for the other on credit, likewise with disparity. For them (the two shaykhs): he sold a weighed item for a non-weighed item because an animal is not weighed, so it is valid however it is. According to Ahmad, it is not valid. It is not valid on credit by consensus. If one buys a live sheep for a slaughtered sheep, it is valid in all of their views. (Ayni, Fath) 'And al-karbas' (with kasra on the kaaf): a garment of white cotton. (Fath) 'For cotton': i.e., the sale of a cotton garment for cotton is valid absolutely, whether equal or with disparity. Similarly, for yarn because their genera differ. If one sells cotton for yarn, it is valid according to Muhammad due to difference of genus because yarn is not unspun to become cotton again. Abu Yusuf said: it is not valid unless equal because yarn of cotton is cotton. The opinion of Muhammad is more apparent. If one sells carded cotton for uncarded cotton, it is valid if one knows that the pure cotton in the carded is more than that in the uncarded; otherwise not. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 59/2, 60] (Fath) 'And fresh dates, etc.': i.e., by measure according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not valid, and the three (other imams) also said this, due to his (peace be upon him) statement when asked about it: 'Does it decrease when it dries?' It was said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Then no.' So he invalidated the sale and indicated the cause: the decrease. So the sale of fresh dates for dry dates is not valid. And his statement (peace be upon him) in the well-known hadith: 'Dates for dates like for like,' and fresh dates are dates, so its sale for dry dates equally is valid. The proof that it is dates is that when fresh dates were presented to him (peace be upon him), he said: 'Is all the dates of Khaybar like this?' He called it dates. And because if it is dates, its sale is permitted by the first part of the hadith: 'Dates for dates like for like.' If it is not dates, then by the latter part of the hadith: 'If the types differ, sell as you wish.' And what they narrated is not authentic as stated. Even if authentic, a solitary report is not used to oppose the well-known. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or for dry dates, etc.': i.e., the sale of fresh dates for dry dates while being equal (by measure). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not valid. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage discusses specific sales that are considered valid in the Hanafi school despite involving apparent disparity. It starts with the ruling that selling a handful for two handfuls is valid because a handful does not fall under a standard measure (like weight or volume), so the cause of riba (which is measured equality) is absent. Similarly, an apple for two apples, an egg for two eggs, etc., are valid due to the same reasoning. However, there is a reservation from Imam Muhammad that such transactions are disliked (makruh) because they could be a means to larger riba. Next, the sale of a fals (a low-value coin) for two fals is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because fals are considered by convention as commodities, not pure currency; Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i disagree, considering fals as money. The sale of meat for a live animal is valid according to the two shaykhs because meat is weighed and animal is not, so they are of different genera; Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i restrict this to same genus only if the meat is more than the animal's meat. The sale of a cotton cloth for cotton is valid absolutely because their genera differ (cloth vs. raw cotton). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about selling cotton for yarn. Finally, the sale of fresh dates (rutab) for dry dates (tamr) is valid according to Abu Hanifa provided they are equal in measure (kil bi kil), because fresh dates are considered dates and thus fall under the hadith 'dates for dates like for like'; the Sahibayn and the three other imams prohibit it due to the decrease upon drying. The commentary provides detailed proofs and responses to objections.

Key Terms
الحفنة (al-hafnah): A handful, i.e., the amount held in two cupped hands, not a standard weight or volume measure.
الفلس (al-fals): A low-value coin, historically a copper coin, considered a commodity by convention rather than pure currency.
الرطب (al-rutab): Fresh, moist dates that still contain water content.
متماثلاً (mutamāthilan): Equal in measure (when selling the same genus). Here specifically 'kaylan bi kayl' (by volume).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is selling a handful for two handfuls valid?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly allowed it
B) Because a handful does not fall under a standard measure, so the cause of riba is absent
C) Because it is a sale of the same genus
D) Because it involves immediate payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits six specific sales that appear to involve riba due to the absence of the effective cause, conventions, or differences in genus, with careful conditions and internal disputes.

Selling a Fals for Two Fals: Custom Overrides Price Status
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fals and the condition of specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling a fals for two fals when specified, and identify the condition of identity required by all.

Core Idea
Fals is not inherently a price; custom can remove its thamaniyya, allowing sale for more than its like when specified.

The sale of a handful for two handfuls, a walnut for two walnuts, a date for two dates, and a copper coin (fals) for two fals is permissible according to the two shaykhs (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf), provided the items are specifically identified. This ruling hinges on the nature of fals and the role of custom, contrasting with the view of Imam Muḥammad and al-Shāfiʿī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a fals for two fals. According to the matn of Kanz al-Daqāʾiq, the ruling is permissibility, but with a condition.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muḥammad prohibits it; the two shaykhs (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) allow it. Al-Shāfiʿī agrees with Muḥammad.
3
Understand the opposing proof
Muḥammad argues: fals is a price (thaman) like dirhams, so selling one for two of its kind entails ribā al-faḍl, which is prohibited. Specification does not change this.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The two shaykhs respond: fals is not inherently a price; it becomes a price by custom. People customarily treat fals as a commodity, so its thamaniyya can be lifted by mutual agreement. Dirhams are different because they were created as prices.
5
Note the essential condition
All scholars agree that the items must be specifically identified (bi aʿyānihimā). Without specification, the sale is unanimously invalid because it leads to ribā.
This condition applies to all similar transactions (handful, walnut, date, etc.).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجوزة بالجوزتين، والتمرة بالتمرتين، والفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما. والفلس: أي وصح أيضاً بيع الفلس بالفلسين. بأعيانهما : [ أي صح الحفنة بالحفنتين حال كونهما معينين. (عيني،مسكين)] يتعلق بجملة هذه المسائل من بيع الحفنة إلى بيع الفلس، وقال محمد: لا يجوز بيع الفلس بالفلسين؛ لأنه ثمن فصار كالدراهم بالدرهمين فلا يتعين بالتعيين، وبه قال الشافعي، وللشيخين: أنه باصطلاح الناس، وقد خرج عن الثمنية باصطلاح المتعاقدين تصحيحاً لتصرفهما بخلاف الدراهم؛ لأنها خلقت ثمناً، وإنما قيد بقوله : بأعيانهما ؛ لأنهما إذا كانا بغير أعيانهما أو أحدهما بغير عينه لا يجوز اتفاقاً؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا.
And a walnut for two walnuts, and a date for two dates, and a fals for two fals with their specific identities. And al-fals: meaning the sale of a fals for two fals is also valid. 'With their specific identities': [meaning the sale of a handful for two handfuls is valid while they are specific. (ʿAynī, Miskīn)] pertains to all these issues from the sale of a handful to the sale of a fals. And Muḥammad said: It is not permissible to sell a fals for two fals because it is a price and thus becomes like dirhams for two dirhams and is not specified by specification. Al-Shāfiʿī also said this. And for the two shaykhs: It is based on the custom of the people, and it has exited the status of being a price by the custom of the contracting parties to validate their transaction, unlike dirhams because they were created as a price. The restriction 'with their specific identities' was mentioned because if they are without their specific identities, or one of them without its specific identity, it is not permissible by consensus because it leads to ribā.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of a fals (copper coin) for two fals, alongside similar transactions like a handful for two handfuls. The core issue is whether fals retains its nature as a price (thaman), like a silver dirham, which would prohibit selling it for more than its equivalent. Imam Muḥammad (and al-Shāfiʿī) argue that fals is a price, analogous to dirhams, and thus cannot be sold for two of its kind without immediate exchange and equality. The two shaykhs (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) counter that fals's status as a price is not inherent but defined by custom; people commonly trade it as a commodity, so it can exit the thaman category through mutual agreement. The condition 'with their specific identities' (bi aʿyānihimā) is crucial—if the items are not specified, the sale is unanimously invalid due to ribā. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that of the shaykhs, permitting the sale with specification.

Key Terms
فلس (fals): A low-value copper coin used in markets; not a pure price like dirhams.
ثمنية (thamaniyya): The quality of being a price (thaman) that prevents selling it for more than its like.
بأعيانهما (bi a'yānihimā): Identified and specified; the two items are known and selected at time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition is essential for the validity of selling a fals for two fals according to all Hanafi scholars?
A) The falses must be of the same weight.
B) The falses must be specified (bi a'yānihimā).
C) The sale must be on credit (nasīʾah).
D) The falses must be from different batches.

Key Takeaway
Custom can override the price status of fals, allowing its sale for more than its like, provided the items are specifically identified to avoid ribā.

Selling Meat for an Animal: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on selling meat for an animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on selling meat for an animal and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Meat is weighed, animal is not; thus the two shaykhs allow the sale unconditionally, while Muhammad restricts same-species exchanges.

This lesson examines an important fiqh dispute within the Hanafi school: the sale of a weighed commodity (meat) for a live animal. The two shaykhs, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, consider this exchange permissible in all cases, while Imam Muhammad imposes a condition for same-species transactions. Understanding the basis of each position sharpens our ability to apply the principles of riba and the legal classification of commodities.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (two shaykhs)
Identify the ruling given by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: unconditional permissibility for selling meat for an animal, whether same species or different.
The key argument: the animal is not weighed (mawzun), so riba al-fadl does not apply.
2
Identify the opposing position (Muhammad and al-Shafi'i)
Learn that Muhammad and al-Shafi'i restrict same-species exchanges: they require the meat to exceed what is in the animal, to avoid ambiguity.
They rely on a hadith prohibition and the principle of genus equivalence.
3
Analyze Muhammad's proof
Examine the hadith narrated by Malik and the qiyas argument: if credit sale is prohibited due to same genus, so is spot sale with disparity.
The hadith: the Prophet prohibited selling meat for an animal.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the counter-argument: the sale is of a weighed item for a non-weighed item, so the prohibition of riba al-fadl does not apply.
Animals are sold by count, not weight, thus the 'illa (effective cause) of riba is absent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Identify the practical consequence: under the two shaykhs' view, any sale of meat for an animal is valid; under Muhammad's, same-species must have excess. The relied-upon fatwa is that of the two shaykhs.
Also note consensus on prohibition of credit sales in this exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللحم بالحيوان. واللحم إلخ : أي وصح بيع اللحم بالحيوان عند الشيخين، سواء كان من جنسه بأن باع لحم شاة بشاة، أو من خلاف جنسه بأن باع لحم بعير بشاة، وقال محمد والشافعي: لا يصح بيع اللحم بالحيوان من جنسه إلا أن يكون اللحم المفرز أكثر من الذي في الحيوان ليكون اللحم بمقابلة ما فيه من اللحم والباقي بالسقط؛ لنهيه عليه عن بيع اللحم بالحيوان، رواه مالك في "الموطأ"، ولأنهما جنس واحد، ولهذا لا يجوز بيع أحدهما بالآخر نسيئة فكذا متفاضلا ولهما أنه باع الموزون بغير الموزون؛ لأن الحيوان ليس بموزون، فيجوز كيف ما كان، وعند أحمد: لا يجوز، ولا يجوز بالنسيئة إجماعاً، ولو اشترى شاة حية بشاة مذبوحة يجوز في قولهم جميعا.
And meat for an animal. And meat etc.: meaning the sale of meat for an animal is valid according to the two shaykhs, whether it is from the same species, such as selling the meat of a sheep for a sheep, or from a different species, such as selling camel meat for a sheep. And Muḥammad and al-Shāfiʿī said: It is not valid to sell meat for an animal of the same species unless the separated meat is more than what is in the animal, so that the meat is in exchange for the meat in it, and the remainder is for the parts not meat, because of the Prophet's prohibition against selling meat for an animal, as narrated by Mālik in al-Muwaṭṭaʾ, and because they are one genus, and for this reason it is not permissible to sell one for the other on credit, and likewise with excess. And for the two shaykhs: He sold a weighed item for a non-weighed item, because an animal is not weighed, so it is permissible in any manner. According to Aḥmad, it is not permissible. It is not permissible on credit by consensus. And if one buys a live sheep for a slaughtered sheep, it is permissible in the opinion of all of them.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling meat (which is weighed, mawzūn) for a live animal (which is not weighed). The two shaykhs (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) deem it permissible regardless of whether the species match or differ, because the meat is a weighed commodity while the animal is not, so the prohibition of ribā due to inequality does not apply. Muḥammad and al-Shāfiʿī restrict this: if the meat and animal are from the same species (e.g., sheep meat for a sheep), it is only valid if the meat is clearly more than the meat estimated in the animal, to avoid ambiguity; otherwise, it is prohibited due to a ḥadīth. The text notes consensus on prohibition of credit sales in such exchanges. However, everyone agrees that a live sheep bought for a slaughtered sheep is permissible, as both are considered animals (hayawān). The relied-upon position in the Ḥanafī school is that of the two shaykhs, allowing the sale with or without excess.

Key Terms
موزون (mawzūn): A commodity sold by weight, such as meat.
حيوان (hayawān): Live animal, not sold by weight but by count or head.
الشيخين (al-shaykhayn): The two shaykhs: Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying cause ('illa) that the two shaykhs use to justify the permissibility of selling meat for an animal?
A) The animal is sold by weight.
B) The animal is not sold by weight, so ribā does not apply.
C) The meat is not weighed.
D) The Prophet explicitly allowed it.

Key Takeaway
Selling meat (weighed) for a live animal (non-weighed) is permissible according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, regardless of species or disparity in quantity.

Selling Grapes for Grapes and for Raisins: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Riba and Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proof via istihsan and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exchanging grapes for raisins, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule of istihsan in analogous cases.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits exchanging grapes for raisins weight-for-weight by istihsan, despite the Sahibayn's qiyas-based prohibition.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can fresh grapes be exchanged for dried raisins? While all three imams agree on grapes-for-grapes, they differ on grapes-for-raisins. Abu Hanifa analogizes to the permissibility of fresh dates for dried dates, relying on istihsan to override strict qiyas. The Sahibayn see it as akin to prohibited exchanges like fried wheat for unfried wheat. The commentary reports that Imam Muhammad initially permitted but later retracted, strengthening Abu Hanifa's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and positions
Determine which transactions are agreed upon (grapes for grapes) and which are disputed (grapes for raisins). Note the three imams' views: Abu Hanifa permits; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prohibit. Also note Muhammad's retraction.
The retraction is reported by Hisham and is significant for tarjih.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to analogize to the permissibility of fresh dates for dried dates. This is based on the idea that both fresh and dried forms are the same genus, and practical necessity (drying) justifies the exchange weight-for-weight.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here; the qiyas would forbid due to potential riba al-fadl.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn apply qiyas and forbid the exchange, comparing it to fried wheat for unfried wheat, where processing changes the item's nature and equality cannot be assured.
They see grapes and raisins as significantly different after drying.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The text concludes that Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi due to the strength of istihsan and because Muhammad's retraction (under pressure) does not negate the original permissibility. Also, common practice supports it.
Al-Marghinani and later Hanafi authorities prefer Abu Hanifa.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi following Abu Hanifa can exchange grapes for raisins weight-for-weight; one following the Sahibayn must avoid it. In fatwa practice, the first is relied upon.
This flexibility shows how istihsan accommodates trade needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعنب بالعنب وبالزبيب أي صح بيع العنب بالعنب مطلقاً علم التفاوت بعد الجفاف أو لا [ أي صح بيع العنب بالزبيب كبيع الرطب بالتمر متماثلاً في الوزن عند أبي حنيفة، خلافاً لهما، والتعليل ما مر، وقيل: لا يجوز بالاتفاق كالحنطة المقلية بغير المقلية وذكر أبو جعفر جواز بيع أحدهما بالآخر في قولهم جميعاً، وروي هشام عن محمد أنه قال: لا بأس بقفيز من العنب بقفيز من الزبيب فألزمته الرطب بالتمر، ولم أزل به حتى رجع محمد عن قوله، وأما العنب بالعنب فيجوز في قولهم جميعاً كالرطب بالرطب، وأما البسر بالرطب، والرطب بالتمر، والعنب بالزبيب فيجوز مثلاً بمثل عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: لا يجوز.
And [the sale of] grapes for grapes and for raisins: meaning the sale of grapes for grapes is valid absolutely, whether the difference after drying is known or not. [Meaning the sale of grapes for raisins is valid like the sale of fresh dates for dried dates, weight for weight, according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The reasoning is as previously mentioned. And it is said it is not permissible by consensus, like fried wheat with unfried wheat. And Abu Ja'far mentioned the permissibility of selling one for the other in all their statements. And Hisham narrated from Muhammad that he said: There is no harm in a qafiz of grapes for a qafiz of raisins, so I compelled him [by analogy] to [the case of] fresh dates for dried dates, and I did not cease until Muhammad retracted his statement. As for grapes for grapes, it is permissible in all their statements like fresh dates for fresh dates. As for unripe dates for fresh dates, fresh dates for dried dates, and grapes for raisins, it is permissible like for like according to Abu Hanifa, but they (the two) said: it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of exchanging fresh grapes for dried raisins. The author clarifies that Abu Hanifa allows such exchange weight-for-weight, analogizing it to the sale of fresh dates for dried dates, which is permitted by istihsan despite the potential increase in weight after drying. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) prohibit it, considering it like the prohibited sale of fried wheat for unfried wheat. The commentary reports a narration from Hisham ibn Bishr that Imam Muhammad initially permitted it but later retracted. The ruling is that grapes for grapes are universally permissible; grapes for raisins are permissible only at weight parity according to Abu Hanifa, while the other views are also transmitted. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa due to the strength of istihsan.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Unlawful increase in exchange of two similar items measured by weight or volume, forbidden in Shari'ah.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juridical preference overriding qiyas for a stronger evidence or need.
المثل بمثل (al-mithlu bi-mithl): Equal in weight or measure, required for riba-free exchange of same genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Abu Hanifa permits it weight-for-weight; the Sahibayn prohibit it.
C) Abu Hanifa prohibits it; the Sahibayn permit it.
D) The ruling is disputed but all agree it is permissible if equal by volume.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view, supported by istihsan, permits exchanging fresh grapes for dried raisins weight-for-weight, and this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Selling Meats of Different Genera: Conditions and Genus Definition
Ruling with conditions and genus definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid sale of meats from different genera and apply the Hanafi rule of genus identification based on zakah nisab completion.

Core Idea
Meats of different genera can be swapped with disparity; same genus demands equality unless it's bird meat.

This passage expands the riba rules on weighable items to meat sales. Unlike other schools that treat all meats as one genus, the Hanafis define genus through the zakah nisab rule: animals whose nisab cannot be combined are different genera. This determines when unequal exchange is allowed. Bird meat is exempt because it isn't customarily weighed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of meats from different animal genera with disparity (one more than the other). The ruling: it is valid (sahih) with two conditions.
The general principle is that exchange of weighable items of same genus must be equal to avoid riba.
2
State the two conditions
First, payment must be spot (naqdan), not credit. Second, the meats must be from different genera as defined by Hanafi fiqh.
Credit invalidates the transaction even if genera differ.
3
Understand the Hanafi definition of genus
The genus is determined by the zakah nisab completion rule: if the nisab of one animal type can be completed by another (they combine), they are the same genus; if they do not combine, they are different genera.
Examples of same genus: cow and buffalo, sheep and goat, Arabian and Bactrian camel. Different genera: sheep vs cattle, cattle vs camel.
4
Apply the definition to meat exchange
For same-genre meats (e.g., beef for buffalo), disparity is prohibited – they must be equal. For different-genre meats (e.g., mutton for beef), disparity is allowed with spot payment.
The three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this.
5
Note the bird meat exception
Even if all bird meats are one genus, disparity is allowed because birds are not customarily weighed, so they are not subject to the riba prohibition on weighable items.
This is based on the illa (effective cause) of weighability being absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللحوم أي صح أيضاً بيع اللحوم المختلفة جنسا، وكل ما يكمل به نصاب الآخر من الحيوان في الزكاة يوصف باختلاف الجنس كالغنم والبقر والإبل، وما لا يكون كذلك كالبقر والجواميس يوصف باتحاد الجنس. بعضها إلخ: أي صح بيع اللحوم المختلفة جنساً بعضها ببعض متفاضلاً مثل لحم الشاة بلحم البقر، ولحم الجاموس بلحم الجمل، ولكن بشرطين أحدهما أن يكون نقداً لا نسيئة، والثاني: أن يكون مختلفة الجنس، ولحم الجاموس والبقر جنس واحد، وكذا لحم المعز مع الضأن، حتى لو باع لحم جاموس بلحم البقر، ولحم ضأن بلحم المعز، ولحم العراب بلحم البخاتي لم يجز متفاضلاً؛ لاتحاد أجناسهما، فتم علة حرمة الفضل، وعند الثلاثة: لا يجوز مطلقاً إلا إذا تساوى البدلان بخلاف بيع لحم الطير بجنسه متفاضلاً حيث يجوز مع اتحاد الجنس؛ لأنه لا يوزن عادة، فليس بوزني ولا كيلي، فلم يتناوله القدر الشرعي، فيجوز متفاضلاً.
And meats: meaning the sale of meats of different genera is also valid. And every [animal] by which the nisab of another animal is completed in zakah is described as being of different genus, like sheep, cattle, and camels. And what is not like that, such as cattle and buffaloes, is described as being of the same genus. Some of them etc.: meaning the sale of meats of different genera, one for the other with disparity, is valid, such as mutton for beef, and buffalo meat for camel meat, but with two conditions: one that it be in cash, not on credit, and the second that they be of different genera. And buffalo and cattle meat are one genus, likewise goat and sheep meat, so if one sold buffalo meat for cattle meat, or sheep meat for goat meat, or Arabian camel meat for Bactrian camel meat, it is not permissible with disparity due to the unity of their genera, thus the cause for prohibition of excess is established. And according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad): it is absolutely not permissible except if the two replacements are equal. Unlike the sale of bird meat with its genus with disparity, which is permissible despite unity of genus, because it is not usually weighed, so it is not a weighable nor a measurable item, thus it is not covered by the legal ruling, so it is permissible with disparity.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of different meats. The author explains that meats from different animal genera can be exchanged with disparity (one more than the other) provided two conditions: 1) the transaction is spot (cash), not credit; 2) the meats are from different genera. The genus is determined by the rule of zakah: animals that do not complete each other's nisab are different genera, e.g., sheep and cattle. Those that do complete are same genus, e.g., cattle and buffalo, sheep and goat, Arab and Bactrian camels. If the genera are the same, disparity is not allowed. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) all agree on this condition. A special case is bird meat: even of the same genus, disparity is allowed because birds are not customarily weighed, thus the riba prohibition on weighable items does not apply.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): Genus of animal as determined by zakah nisab completion rule.
متفاضلاً (mutafāḍilan): Unequal in quantity, allowed only when genera differ.
نقداً (naqdan): Spot payment, immediate exchange without credit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition allows selling beef with mutton in unequal amounts?
A) They must be equal in weight
B) Payment must be on credit
C) The meats must be from different genera
D) It is never allowed

Key Takeaway
The validity of exchanging meats with disparity depends on genus (defined by zakah rules) and spot payment; bird meat is an exception.

Genus Rules for Exchanging Milk, Vinegar, Fat, and Bread
Rulings for exchange of milk, vinegar, fat, and bread with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an exchange of milk, vinegar, fat, or bread is valid by applying the Hanafi principle of genus difference and equality conditions.

Core Idea
Different genus allows disparity; same genus requires exact equality and hand-to-hand delivery.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents specific exchange scenarios involving milk, vinegar, fat, bread, and their derivatives. The underlying principle is the Hanafi doctrine of riba al-fadl: when two items are from the same genus, they must be equal in measure and exchanged hand-to-hand. When they belong to different genera, disparity is allowed as long as the transaction is spot. Understanding these classifications helps avoid prohibited riba in everyday food exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items being exchanged
Read the passage and list each pair: cow's milk for sheep's milk, vinegar of inferior dates for vinegar of grapes, belly fat for tail fat or meat, bread for wheat or flour, wheat for flour or sawiq, olives for oil, sesame for sesame oil, and borrowing bread by weight or count.
Some pairs are allowed with disparity, others are prohibited.
2
Determine if the pair belongs to the same genus (jins) in Hanafi law
Apply the Hanafi criteria: items sharing the same essence and primary purpose are same genus. Cow's milk and sheep's milk are different genera because the animal source differs. Vinegars from different bases are different genera. Fats: belly fat, tail fat, and meat are considered different genera due to different forms and uses. Bread is a different genus from wheat and flour because it is transformed. Wheat, flour, and sawiq are same genus (all from wheat). Olives and olive oil are same genus; sesame and sesame oil are same genus.
Genus determination is key. The Hanafi view uses detailed criteria unlike other schools.
3
Apply the appropriate ruling for each pair
If different genera: sale is valid with disparity, but must be hand-to-hand (spot). If same genus: sale must be equal in measure and spot. For wheat-flour/sawiq: prohibited because even if weighed, the density difference makes exact equality impossible. For oil extraction: allowed only if the oil amount exceeds the oil content of the fruit, otherwise it resembles same-genus exchange with disparity.
The text explicitly allows disparity for different genera, e.g., bread for wheat with disparity.
4
Analyze the special case of borrowing bread
Borrowing bread (qard) must be by weight, not count, to ensure repayment of an equivalent amount. This follows the principle that bread differs in size and density, so counting is insufficient. Abu Hanifa prohibited such loans entirely, but Abu Yusuf allowed by weight, and Muhammad allowed by weight or count. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.
Intra-Hanafi dispute: see al-mukhtalaf fih.
5
Summarize the practical fatwa for each exchange
From the text and commentary: cow milk for sheep milk – valid with disparity; vinegar of dates for vinegar of grapes – valid with disparity; belly fat for tail fat or meat – valid with disparity; bread for wheat or flour – valid with disparity but spot; wheat for flour or sawiq – invalid; olives for oil / sesame for oil – valid only if oil > fruit's oil content; borrowing bread – valid by weight, not count (mufta bihi).
All these are transactions in food items subject to riba rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبن البقر بلبن الغنم، وخلّ الدقل بخل العنب وشحم البطن بالألية أو باللحم والخبز بالبر والدقيق متفاضلاً، لا بيع البُرّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، والزيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالشيرج، حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم، ويستقرض الخبز وزناً لا عدداً.
And [the sale of] cow's milk for sheep's milk, and vinegar of inferior dates for vinegar of grapes, and belly fat for tail fat or for meat, and bread for wheat and flour with disparity; not [valid is] the sale of wheat for flour or for sawiq, and olive for oil, and sesame for sesame oil, unless the oil is more than what is in the olives and sesame; and bread may be borrowed by weight, not by count.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage lists several rulings for riba-related exchanges. Each item is considered a different genus from its counterpart, allowing disparity with spot payment. Cow milk and sheep milk are different genera due to different animal origins. Vinegar of inferior dates and vinegar of grapes are different genera due to different base materials. Belly fat, tail fat, and meat are considered different genera even if from the same animal, because of different names, forms, and purposes. Bread is a different genus from wheat and flour, and can be exchanged with disparity but only spot. However, selling wheat for flour or sawiq is absolutely prohibited because they are parts of the same genus, and equality cannot be ensured due to different densities. Olive for olive oil and sesame for sesame oil require that the extracted oil be more than the oil contained in the fruit to avoid riba. Finally, borrowing bread must be by weight, not count, to ensure equality; there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on this.

Key Terms
الخل (al-khall): Vinegar, a condiment made from dates or grapes.
الدقل (al-daqal): Inferior dates used for making vinegar.
السويق (al-sawīq): Parched barley or wheat ground into flour or coarse meal.
الألية (al-aliyya): Tail fat (of a sheep).
الشحم (al-shahm): Fat, here specifically belly fat.
الزيتون (al-zaytūn): Olives (fruit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are different genera.
B) They are the same genus and equality cannot be ensured due to density differences.
C) Flour is not considered a food item.
D) The prohibition is only if the exchange is on credit.

Key Takeaway
When exchanging food items, first determine if they are the same genus; if yes, require exact equality and hand-to-hand; if not, disparity is allowed but must be spot.

Selling Wheat for Flour or Sawiq: Riba Rules
Ruling on exchange of wheat with its processed forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wheat for flour or sawiq and explain the basis of the intra-madhhab dispute with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Wheat and its derivatives are one genus; exchange must be equal to avoid riba.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the prohibition of selling wheat (burr) for its processed derivatives, flour (daqiq) and sawiq (parched flour). The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, treats these as the same genus for riba purposes, making unequal exchange impermissible. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that difference in name and processing creates separate genera. Understanding this ruling is essential for applying riba prohibitions in commodity exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited transactions
The text states that selling wheat for flour or sawiq is not permissible, whether equal or unequal. Why?
2
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
The prohibition is based on the fact that both flour and sawiq are parts of wheat, but their different densities make exact equality in measure impossible, leading to gharar (uncertainty) and potential riba.
3
Distinguish the ruling for flour-for-flour exchange
Selling flour for flour is allowed only if equal by measure and both flours are compressed and of same fineness. Impermissible by weight or if different fineness.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute over flour vs. sawiq
Abu Hanifa prohibits it absolutely; the Sahibayn permit it because they consider them different genera. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: Abu Hanifa rules invalid, Sahibayn valid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position (al-ashahh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الربا بالبر والدقيق متفاضلاً، لا بيع البُرّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، والزيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالشيرج، حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم، أي ولا بيع السمسم ويستقرض الخبز وزناً لا عدداً. أي من حيث الوزن وبين أي الخالص أي ! الخالص لا بيع البر إلخ: أي لا يجوز بيع الحنطة بالدقيق أو بالسويق لا متفاضلاً ولا متساوياً؛ لأنهما من أجزاء الحنطة، والكيل غير مسو بينهما الحنطة؛ لاكتنازهما، وتخلخل حبات الحنطة فصار كالمجازفة في احتمال الزيادة فلا يجوز وإن كان كيلاً بكيل، ويجوز بيع الدقيق بالدقيق متساوياً كيلاً، ولا يجوز متفاضلاً؛ لاتحاد الاسم والصورة والمعنى، وإنما يجوز بيع الدقيق بالدقيق إذا كانا مكبوسين، وكانا على صفة واحدة من النعومة، وإن كانا غير مكبوسين أو أحدهما لا يجوز، وإن باع الدقيق بالدقيق موازنة، ففيه روايتان والأصح عدم الجواز، ولا يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق لا متساوياً ولا متفاضلاً عند أبي حنيفة؛ إذ السويق أجزاء الحنطة المقلية، والدقيق أجزاء الحنطة الغير المقلية، الحنطة المقلية بغير المقلية لا بحال، فكذا أجزائهما، وقالا يجوز كيف ما كان؛ لأنهما وبيع يصح بيع قلته فأمثلته العقد هو جنسان مختلفان لاختلاف الاسم والمقصود وله أنهما جنس واحد من وجه. (عيني، فتح، كافي) أو بالسويق: سواء كان متساويا أو متفاضلا؛ لأنه جنس واحد من وجه وإن خص باسم آخر. (عيني)
Chapter of Riba: With wheat and flour in unequal amounts, it is not [permissible] to sell wheat for flour or for sawiq, and olives for oil, and sesame for shiraj (sesame oil), unless the oil or shiraj is more than what is in the olives or sesame, and it is not [permissible] to sell sesame. And one may borrow bread by weight, not by count. That is, by weight. And between... [unclear]... The explanation: It says: It is not permissible to sell wheat for flour or sawiq, whether equal or unequal, because both are parts of wheat, and the measure is not equal between them due to density and looseness of wheat grains, so it becomes like selling by guesswork with potential surplus, hence not permissible even if measure for measure. Selling flour for flour is permissible equally by measure, but not unequally due to unity of name, form, and meaning. Flour for flour is permissible only if both are compressed and of the same fineness; if not compressed or one is not, it is not permissible. If flour is sold by weight, there are two narrations, the correct view being impermissibility. Selling flour for sawiq is not permissible equal or unequal according to Abu Hanifa, because sawiq is roasted parts of wheat and flour is unroasted, and roasted wheat with unroasted is not [permissible] at all, so likewise their parts. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is permissible however it is, because they are two different genera due to difference in name and purpose, but for him (Abu Hanifa) they are one genus from one aspect ('Ayni, Fath, Kafi). Or for sawiq: whether equal or unequal, because it is one genus from one aspect even if named differently ('Ayni).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on riba. The text states the prohibition of selling wheat (burr) for flour (daqiq) or sawiq (parched flour) in unequal or even equal amounts. The reason given in the commentary is that both flour and sawiq are parts of wheat, but they differ in density and volume, making precise equality impossible by measure; thus it resembles gharar (uncertainty) and is prohibited. Selling flour for flour is allowed equally by measure, but not unequally. However, conditions apply: both flours must be compressed and of the same fineness. By weight, there is dispute. For flour and sawiq, Abu Hanifa prohibits absolutely because sawiq is roasted and flour is unroasted, so they are different states of the same genus; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it because they consider them different genera based on name and purpose. The author notes that even if named differently, they are one genus from one aspect, so Abu Hanifa's view is upheld.

Key Terms
البر (al-burr): Wheat grain; the base species for riba considerations.
الدقيق (al-daqiq): Flour of wheat; considered part of the same genus as wheat.
السويق (al-sawiq): Parched flour (roasted wheat grains ground).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, is selling flour (daqiq) for sawiq (parched flour) permissible?
A) Yes, equally by weight.
B) Yes, equally by measure.
C) No, not permissible in any case.
D) Yes, but only if compressed.

Key Takeaway
Wheat and its processed derivatives are one genus; any exchange must be exactly equal to avoid riba, and Abu Hanifa's stricter view is relied upon.

Selling Olives for Oil: The Four Cases and the Zufar Dispute
Ruling with proof: analysis of sale of olives for oil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios for selling olives for oil and identify when the transaction is valid or invalid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
When exchanging a fruit for its extract, the sale is invalid unless the extract in the fruit is less than the extract sold.

This passage addresses a nuanced issue in sales: when a fruit (like olives or sesame) is exchanged for its extracted oil. Because both items are considered the same genus, riba (usury) rules apply: equivalence must be known. The text presents four scenarios and an intra-madhhab dispute on the case of unknown oil quantity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four cases
Identify the three cases settled by consensus: (1) oil in fruit more than exchanged oil → invalid; (2) equal → invalid; (3) exchanged oil more → valid. Note the fourth disputed case when proportions are unknown.
The consensus covers the first three; only the fourth is disputed.
2
Analyze Zufar's view
Zufar rules the fourth case valid, citing the default of permissibility and that doubt should not corrupt a contract.
Zufar's principle: al-asl fil-ashya' al-ibahah.
3
Examine the Hanafi majority argument
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad argue that corruption is dominant (two of the three known outcomes are invalid) and that doubt in riba is treated as certainty.
They use qiyas: riba suspicion invalidates the contract.
4
Identify the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Zufar permits the sale when unknown; the majority prohibits it. The tarjih (mufta bihi) is the majority view.
Fatwa is given according to the three.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a farmer selling olive oil in exchange for the olive paste (thufl) or similar. The same rules apply: if the oil quantity in the paste is unknown, the sale is invalid.
The principle extends to any fruit-extract exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزيتون: أي ولا يصح أيضاً بيع الزيتون. (عيني) والشيرج إلخ : الشيرج معرب، وهو دهن السمسم بكسر السين وحكي فتحها وقيل للدهن الأبيض وللعصير قبل أن يتغير شيرج تشبيها به لصفائه، وهو بفتح الشين مثل زينب ملحق بباب فعلل نحو جعفر ولا يجوز كسر الشين؛ لأنه يصير من باب درهم وهو قليل ومعنا محصورة وليس هذا منها، ثم اعلم أن بيع الزيتون بالزيت والسمسم بالشيرج على أربعة أوجه: إن علم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر من الزيت المنفصل لم يصح، وكذا إن علم أنه مثله وإن كان الزيت المنفصل أكثر جاز، وهذه الثلاثة بالإجماع، وإن لم يعلم أنه مثله أو أكثر منه أو أقل منه صح عند زفر؛ لأن الأصل الجواز، فلا يفسد بالشك والاحتمال ولنا أن جهة الفساد غالبة؛ لأنه يفسد من وجهين، ويصح من وجه واحد، فلا يصح؛ ولأن المتوهم في الربا كالمتحقق، وعند الثلاثة: لا أصلاً. يصح (عيني، فتح) والسمسم: فيكون الدهن بمثله والزيادة بالثفل (ط)
And olives: meaning that selling olives [for oil] is also not valid ('Ayni). And shiraj etc.: shiraj is an arabicized word, meaning sesame oil... Then know that selling olives for oil and sesame for shiraj has four cases: If it is known that the oil in the olives is more than the separated oil, it is not valid; likewise if it is known that it is equal; if the separated oil is more, it is valid. These three are by consensus. If it is not known whether it is equal, more, or less, it is valid according to Zufar because the default is permissibility, so doubt does not corrupt. But for us, the aspect of corruption is dominant because it is invalid from two aspects and valid from one, so it is not valid; and because the suspected in riba is like the actual. And according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), it is not valid at all. ('Ayni, Fath) And sesame: the oil is equivalent and the increase is against the residue (T).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage rules on selling olives for oil and sesame for sesame oil. Four scenarios: (1) If the oil inside the fruit is known to be more than the extracted oil, sale invalid. (2) If equal, also invalid. (3) If extracted oil is more, valid—these three by consensus. (4) If quantity is unknown, Zufar says valid, but Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn say invalid because corruption is dominant; the principle 'doubt in riba is like certainty' applies.

Key Terms
الشيرج (al-shiraj): Sesame oil
الزيت (al-zayt): Olive oil
ثفل (thufl): Residue or pulp left after oil extraction
ربا (riba): Usury or unjustified increase in exchange contracts

C) Valid only if the olives are weighed and equivalent to the oil.
D) Invalid unless the oil is extracted before the sale.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits selling a fruit for its extract when the quantity of extract in the fruit is unknown, because of the preponderance of doubt regarding riba.

The Three Hanafi Views on Borrowing Bread
Ruling on loan (qard) of bread: by weight vs count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on borrowing bread, identify the mufta bihi view, and explain the basis for each position.

Core Idea
Borrowing bread is disputed: Abu Hanifa prohibits entirely, Abu Yusuf permits by weight, Muhammad permits by either.

This passage presents a classical intra-Hanafi dispute on whether bread can be borrowed (qard) and, if so, how. The disagreement hinges on whether bread is considered mithli (fungible) — goods that can be exactly substituted. Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani each have distinct positions, and the later fatwa adopts Abu Yusuf's view. This lesson will walk through the arguments and the practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the three Hanafi positions
List the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the permissibility and method of borrowing bread.
Abu Hanifa: not permissible at all. Abu Yusuf: permissible only by weight. Muhammad: permissible by weight or count.
2
Identify the core argument behind each position
For each imam, identify the reasoning: Abu Hanifa argues bread is not mithli due to inherent variation; Abu Yusuf insists on weight to ensure equality; Muhammad relies on custom and need to override strict analogy.
The arguments involve concepts of mithli, riba, and custom (urf).
3
Understand the proof structures
Recognize that Abu Hanifa uses qiyas (analogy to riba) while Muhammad uses istihsan (juristic preference based on custom). Abu Yusuf strikes a middle ground.
This is a classic clash of qiyas vs. istihsan.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative) position
The passage explicitly states 'wa bihi yufata' — fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's view. Thus, in practice, bread is borrowed by weight only.
Later Hanafi fatwa relies on Abu Yusuf's position.
5
Note cross-madhhab references
The passage mentions that al-Shafi'i has two sayings (one with Abu Yusuf, one with Muhammad), and Ahmad and Malik also have opinions. This shows the dispute extends beyond the Hanafi school.
These external references are used as supporting authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستقرض الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف؛ لأن الوزن يوجب التساوي، والعدد لا يوجبه فيتحقق شبهة الفضل وهو ملحقة بالأصل، وبه قال الشافعي في قول، وبه يفتى، وعند محمد: يستقرض بهما وزناً وعدداً؛ لأن التفاوت ساقط شرعاً للتعامل وحاجة الناس، والقياس يترك بالتعامل، وبه قال أحمد والشافعي في قول، وعند مالك: يعتبر العرف وعند أبي حنيفة: لا يجوز مطلقاً؛ لأنه وإن وزن فهو متفاوت بتفاوت الخبز والخباز والتنور والتقدم والتأخر، والاستقراض إنما يصح في المثلي فلا يثبت التساوي لا وزناً ولا عدداً، فلا يجوز تحرزاً عن الربا رمز الحقائق: ٦٢/٢] (فتح، كافي)
And borrowing bread is by weight, not by count according to Abu Yusuf, because weight ensures equality, count does not, so suspicion of surplus arises, and it is attached to the original. Al-Shafi'i said this in one saying, and it is given fatwa. According to Muhammad: one may borrow both by weight and count because difference is legally waived due to common practice and people's need, and analogy is abandoned due to custom. Ahmad and al-Shafi'i said this in one saying. According to Malik: custom is considered. According to Abu Hanifa: it is not permissible at all because even if weighed, it varies due to differences in bread, baker, oven, time, etc., and borrowing is only valid for mithli (fungible) goods; equality is not established by weight or count, so it is impermissible to avoid riba. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 62/2) (Fath, Kafi).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing bread. The core issue: bread may differ in quality even if same weight. Abu Yusuf allows only by weight; Muhammad allows both weight and count due to custom; Abu Hanifa disallows entirely because bread is not truly fungible. The fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's view.

Key Terms
الاستقراض (al-istiqrad): Borrowing (qard) of a fungible (mithli) item for consumption.
المثلي (al-mithli): Good that can be substituted exactly by weight, measure, or count without significant difference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only by weight.
B) Only by count.
C) By weight or count.
D) Not permissible at all.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi view on borrowing bread is that it must be done by weight, not by count, to avoid the suspicion of riba, adopting Abu Yusuf's position as a balance between strict avoidance and practical need.

No Riba Between Master and Slave
Ruling with conditions and intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that no riba occurs between master and slave, identify the exceptions (mudabbar, umm walad vs. mukatab), and apply the condition regarding the slave's debt according to the Hanafi positions.

Core Idea
Riba requires exchange between two owners; master and slave are one owner — but this unity breaks when the slave is independent (mukatab) or indebted.

In this passage, we examine a foundational ruling of Hanafi fiqh: riba is not established between a master and his slave because the slave's property belongs to the master. However, this rule has conditions and exceptions. The mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) is treated like a free person, so the general rule does not apply. Additionally, if the slave has debt, the jurists differ on whether the rule remains. By understanding the illa (effective cause) of ownership unity, you will be able to apply this ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling and Its Basis
The passage first states: 'There is no riba between the master and his slave.' Identify the rationale: the slave's property belongs to the master, so there is no real exchange between two owners – a necessary element for riba.
The basis is the unity of ownership (milk واحد).
2
Distinguish Between Types of Slaves
The rule applies to mudabbar (promised freedom upon death) and umm walad (mother of master's child). The exception is the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), who is considered like a free person in possession and earnings.
Mukatab is treated as a separate owner for riba purposes.
3
Recognize the Condition of Permission and Debt
The rule applies only if the slave is permitted to trade (ma'dhun) and has no debt that overwhelms his value. If the slave has such debt, the slave's earnings belong to creditors, not the master – breaking the unity of ownership.
Debt must be 'mustaghriq biraqabatihi wa ma fi yadihi' – covering his person and what he possesses.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Indebted Slave
Abu Hanifa holds that if the slave is indebted, the master's ownership over the slave's earnings is severed, so riba may occur. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that creditors' rights attach, creating suspicion (shubha) that prevents the transaction from being clearly free of riba. Al-Muhit states that even with debt, no riba occurs.
Al-Muhit's view is broader: it upholds the general rule despite debt.
5
Conclude with the Preferred View
The text does not explicitly select a preferred view, but al-Muhit's position is often relied upon in later fatwa. Understand that the mufta bihi may vary by context.
For exam purposes, know the rule and the exceptions, and that there is ikhtilaf on the debt condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ربا بين السيد وعبده، ولا بين المسلم واقمه. وعبده الأن ما في يده لمولاه فلا يتحقق الربا. (عيني)] ولو مدبرا أو أم ولد بخلاف المكاتب؛ لأنه صار كالحر يداً وتصرفاً في كسبه وهذا إذا كان العبد مأذونا له ولم يكن عليه دين مستغرق برقبته وما في يده، وإن كان عليه دين لا يجوز؛ لأن ما في يده ليس ملك للمولى عند أبي حنيفة، فصار كالمكاتب، وعندهما: تعلق به حق الغرماء، فلا يعرى عن الشبهة، وفي "المحيط": لا ربا بينهما، وإن كان عليه دين رمز الحقائق: ٦٢/٢] (فتح)
And there is no riba between the master and his slave, nor between the Muslim and his [?]. And the slave now, what is in his hand belongs to his master, so riba is not established. ('Ayni) Even if the slave is mudabbar or umm walad, unlike the mukatab, because he becomes like a free man in possession and disposal of his earnings. This is when the slave is permitted (to trade) and has no debt encompassing his neck and what is in his hand. If he has debt, it is not permissible because what is in his hand is not the master's property according to Abu Hanifa, so he becomes like the mukatab. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the creditors' right is attached to it, so it is not free from suspicion. And in al-Muhit: There is no riba between them even if he has debt. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 62/2) (Fath).
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage states that riba rules do not apply between master and slave because the slave's property is owned by the master. This applies to mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and umm walad (slave mother of master's child), but not to mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) who is like a free man. Conditions: slave must be permitted to trade and have no debt. If indebted, Abu Hanifa says slave's earnings are for creditors, so riba may apply; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say suspicion remains. Al-Muhit says no riba even with debt.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master for manumission upon payment of a sum; considered semi-free.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred to after the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master, becoming free upon his death.
دين مستغرق (dīn mustaghriq): Debt that covers the entire value of the slave and his possessions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Umm walad
C) Mukatab
D) All slaves

Key Takeaway
The unity of ownership between master and slave nullifies riba, but this unity is broken for the mukatab and, according to some Hanafi authorities, for the indebted slave.

No Riba Between Muslim and Harbi in Dar al-Harb
Ruling: no riba between Muslim and enemy combatant in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb, cite the hadith proof, and distinguish the exception when the harbi enters dar al-Islam with aman.

Core Idea
No riba applies with a harbi in dar al-harb because his property is mubah.

Riba (usury) is normally strictly prohibited in Islamic law. However, the Hanafi school recognizes an exceptional situation: when a Muslim trades with an enemy combatant (harbi) in enemy territory (dar al-harb), riba rules are suspended. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof from a marfu' hadith, and the scholarly disagreement about the case when the harbi enters Muslim lands with safe conduct (aman).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on an exchange between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb. The ruling: no riba applies, meaning unequal or deferred exchanges are permissible.
The ruling is explicitly stated: 'لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب'.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith narrated by Makhul (mursal): 'لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب'. Also a rational proof: the harbi's property is mubah (permissible to take).
The hadith is the primary textual evidence; the rational proof reinforces it.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith directly negates the existence of riba. The legal link: because the harbi's property is mubah, the prohibition of riba (which protects property rights) does not apply. Consent of the harbi is sufficient for validity.
The phrase 'بأن كان بلا عذر' suggests no excuse needed; the exchange is lawful.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (intra-Hanafi) and the Shafi'i, Hanbali, and Maliki schools hold that if the harbi enters dar al-Islam with aman, riba applies, analogizing to a musta'min. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is specific and the mubah status is not undone by aman.
The consensus among the companions? The text says 'اتفاقاً' (by consensus) that with aman in dar al-Islam, riba applies.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is the one stated: no riba in dar al-harb, even with a fasid contract. The hadith is taken as determinative, and the opposing analogy is rejected because the mubah status precedes the aman.
The passage ends with a quotation from 'Ayni reinforcing that aman does not retroactively change the mubah status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحربي: أي الكافر الحربي في دار الحرب. ثمه ولو بعقد فاسد؛ لقوله عليه: "لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب". رواه مكحول، ولأن ماله مباح فيحل برضاه بأن كان بلا عذر، وإنما قيد بقوله: "ثمه"؛ لأنه لو دخل دارنا حربي بأمان، فباع منه مسلم درهماً بدرهمين لا يجوز اتفاقاً، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي وأحمد ومالك في رواية صحيحة: يجري الربا بينهما اعتباراً للحربي بالمستأمن منهم في دارنا؛ لأن المسلم التزم بالأمان أن لا يتملك مالهم إلا بالعقد، وهذا العقد فاسد، فلا يفيد الملك الحلال والحجة عليهما ما روينا، ولأن مالهم مباح، وبعقد معصوماً إلا أنه التزم . أن لا يعذرهم ولا يتعرض لما في أيديهم بدون رضاهم، فإذا أخذه برضاهم الأمان لم يصر ملكه بحكم الإباحة السابقة. (عيني، فتح)
The harbi: i.e., the disbelieving enemy in dar al-harb. There, even with a fasid contract, because of his saying: 'There is no riba between the Muslim and the harbi in dar al-harb.' Narrated by Makhul. And because his property is permissible (mubah), so it becomes lawful with his consent, provided it is without excuse. He only qualified it with 'there' because if a harbi enters our dar with aman, and a Muslim sells him a dirham for two dirhams, it is not permissible by consensus. Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik in a sound narration say: riba applies between them, analogizing the harbi to the musta'min from them in our dar, because the Muslim commits with the aman not to take their property except by valid contract, and this contract is fasid, so it does not give lawful ownership. The proof against them is what we narrated, and because their property is mubah, and with a protected contract... but he committed not to exonerate them and not to interfere with what is in their hands without their consent. So if he takes it with their consent, the aman does not make it his ownership by virtue of the previous permissibility. ('Ayni, Fath).
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage rules that no riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb, even with a fasid contract, based on hadith. The reason: the harbi's property is mubah (permissible to take) for the Muslim. However, if the harbi enters Muslim lands with aman (safe conduct), then riba applies by consensus, because the Muslim is bound by the aman to only use proper contracts. Abu Yusuf, Shafi'i, etc., analogize to musta'min; the Hanafi response is that the hadith is specific and the mubah status precedes the contract.

Key Terms
الحربي (al-harbi): An enemy non-Muslim who is not under Muslim protection and resides in dar al-harb.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Muslim rule and where war is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, it is riba and prohibited.
C) Only if by weight.
D) Only if equal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi exception: no riba between Muslim and harbi in dar al-harb because the harbi's property is mubah, but this exception is void when the harbi enters Muslim lands with aman.

No Riba Between Muslim and Harbi in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on riba between a Muslim and a warring non-Muslim in enemy territory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the disagreement with the opposing schools.

Core Idea
No riba applies between Muslim and harbi in dar al-harb because harbi property is mubah.

This lesson examines a specific ruling where the prohibition of riba is lifted: transactions between a Muslim and a warring non-Muslim (harbi) in enemy territory (dar al-harb). The Hanafi school relies on a explicit hadith to permit unequal exchanges, while other schools, including a leading Hanafi disciple, analogize to the protected non-Muslim (musta'min). We will unpack the evidence, the reasoning, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: riba does not occur between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb.
This applies even if the contract is void under normal Islamic law.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
List the scholars who disagree: Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik (in one narration). They hold that riba does apply.
Abu Yusuf is a major Hanafi figure, making this an intra-madhhab issue as well.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand their reasoning: they analogize the harbi to a musta'min (non-Muslim with aman) in Muslim territory. Since the aman protects property, any contract must be valid; a riba contract is void, so it cannot transfer ownership lawfully.
The key premise: the aman commits the Muslim to only acquire property through valid contracts.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects the analogy because the hadith is explicit and the harbi's property is originally mubah. The aman only prevents taking without consent, but does not change the fundamental permissibility. Thus, with consent, the property is acquired lawfully despite a defective contract.
The Hanafi rely on the general principle that mubah property becomes owned by taking with consent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to Hanafi, a Muslim may exchange unequal amounts of currency with a harbi in dar al-harb with his consent. According to opponents, it is riba and prohibited.
This ruling is specific to dar al-harb; it does not apply to dhimmis or musta'minin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحربي: أي الكافر الحربي في دار الحرب. ثمه ولو بعقد فاسد؛ لقوله عليه: "لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب". رواه مكحول، ولأن ماله مباح فيحل برضاه بأن كان بلا عذر، وإنما قيد بقوله: "ثمه"؛ لأنه لو دخل دارنا حربي بأمان، فباع منه مسلم درهماً بدرهمين لا يجوز اتفاقاً، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي وأحمد ومالك في رواية صحيحة: يجري الربا بينهما اعتباراً للحربي بالمستأمن منهم في دارنا؛ لأن المسلم التزم بالأمان أن لا يتملك مالهم إلا بالعقد، وهذا العقد فاسد، فلا يفيد الملك الحلال والحجة عليهما ما روينا، ولأن مالهم مباح، وبعقد معصوماً إلا أنه التزم . أن لا يعذرهم ولا يتعرض لما في أيديهم بدون رضاهم، فإذا أخذه برضاهم الأمان لم يصر ملكه بحكم الإباحة السابقة. (عيني، فتح)
Al-Harbi: meaning the warring disbeliever in dar al-harb. Then, even with a void contract; due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no riba between the Muslim and the harbi in dar al-harb.' Narrated by Makhul. And because his property is mubah, so it is permissible with his consent, provided it is without [the Muslim having] an excuse. He only restricted by saying 'then' (thumma) because if a harbi enters our territory with aman (safe conduct), and a Muslim sells him a dirham for two dirhams, it is not allowed by consensus. Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik in a sound narration say: riba runs between them, analogizing the harbi to the musta'min (one with aman) among them in our territory; because the Muslim, by the aman, commits not to take their property except by contract, and this contract is void, so it does not effect permissible ownership. The proof against them is what we narrated, and because their property is mubah, and by the contract it is protected, except that he committed not to harm them nor to interfere with what is in their hands without their consent. So if he takes it with their consent, due to the aman, it does not become his property by virtue of the previous permissibility. (Al-`Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule that riba does not occur between a Muslim and a harbi (non-Muslim at war with Islam) in dar al-harb (enemy territory). The proof is a hadith narrated by Makhul. The property of a harbi is considered mubah (ownerless/permissible to take) for Muslims, so exchanging money with excess does not fall under the prohibition. However, this applies only in dar al-harb; if a harbi enters Muslim territory with aman (safe conduct), then riba is prohibited by consensus. The text then presents a disagreement: Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik (in one narration) hold that riba applies to the harbi by analogy with the musta'min (protected person) in our territory, because the aman commits the Muslim to acquire property only through valid contracts; a void contract would not give permissible ownership. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is explicit, and the harbi's property remains mubah in principle; the aman only prevents taking without consent but does not change the fundamental permissibility. Therefore, if the harbi consents, the property is acquired lawfully even with a void contract.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Islam and resides in dar al-harb.
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who enters Muslim territory with a temporary safe conduct (aman).
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under the control of non-Muslims who are at war with Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi evidence for no riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb?
A) Consensus of the Companions
B) A hadith narrated by Makhul
C) A verse from the Quran
D) Qiyas with the musta'min

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that riba does not apply in dar al-harb between a Muslim and a harbi, based on an explicit hadith and the principle that a harbi's property is mubah.
